Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri: Their Role and Significance
Bibliographical list of commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri and explain the purpose of a tashih work in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Commentaries and tashih works are the tools to find the mufta bihi — the operative Hanafi ruling.

This passage from al-Maydānī's al-Lubāb lists several commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Qudūrī, a foundational Hanafī fiqh primer. Among them is a tashīh work by Qāsim ibn Quṭlūbughā that identifies the muftā bihī (relied‑upon) positions. Understanding these genres helps you navigate the Hanafī literary tradition and know which ruling to follow in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main text and its importance
Mukhtasar al-Qudūrī is a concise Hanafī fiqh text used as a beginner's manual. Recognize its central role in the school.
Many later works are built upon it.
2
Categorize each listed work
The passage lists seven works: six commentaries (sharh) and one tashīh. Note the type and author information for each.
Some authors are unknown; others are identified.
3
Understand the function of 'tashīh'
Tashīh means correction or selection. Qāsim ibn Quṭlūbughā wrote his Tashīh al-Qudūrī to clarify which rulings are muftā bihī (operative for fatwa). This is a key tool for later practice.
Tashīh works are essential for knowing the authoritative view.
4
Connect the list to your study of Hanafī fiqh
When studying a ruling, you should consult a commentary or a tashīh to verify the muftā bihī position, not just any opinion.
This list shows the scholarly effort to preserve and select the soundest views.

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographical list from the front matter of al-Lubab, listing commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The author, al-Maydani, enumerates seven works: (63) an anonymous commentary by Muhammad ibn Abdullah (d. before 843 AH), clarifying a misattribution to Abdullah ibn Muhammad; (64) al-Kashf by an unknown author (d. before 856 AH) with two manuscript locations; (65) Fawa'id al-Quduri by an unknown author (d. before 861 AH), noted by Kamal ibn al-Humam for its discussion on qabd (possession) in exchange; (66) al-Jawahir by an unknown author (d. before 879 AH), with a copy in the Library of Congress; and (67) Tashih al-Quduri by Qasim ibn Qutlubugha (d. 879 AH), written to identify the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) positions within the Hanafi school. The list shows the scholarly activity around al-Quduri's text and provides manuscript references.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A concise Hanafi fiqh text by Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), widely studied as a beginner's manual.
شرح (sharh): A commentary explaining a base text (matn).
تصحيح (tashih): A work that corrects and prefers certain rulings in the school, indicating the mufta bihi position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of Qasim ibn Qutlubugha's 'Tashih al-Quduri' according to the passage?
A) To provide a new commentary on every chapter
B) To clarify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) positions in the Hanafi school
C) To refute the Shafi'i school
D) To list all known manuscripts of al-Quduri

Key Takeaway
Commentaries and tashih works are essential for identifying the operative rulings (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school.

Anonymous Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri: Fawa'id and Jawahir
Listing two anonymous commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two anonymous Hanafi commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri and state their significance as sources cited by later Hanafi authorities.

Core Idea
Anonymous commentaries preserve early Hanafi scholarship; their citations by later masters show their authority.

This passage introduces two commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri whose authors are unknown. Despite their anonymity, they were used by major Hanafi jurists like Ibn al-Humam and Qasim ibn Qutlubugha. Understanding these sources helps us trace the transmission of Hanafi fiqh through early commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first commentary
Fawa'id al-Quduri is an anonymous commentary, with its author dying before 861 AH. It is cited by Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir.
This shows that even without knowing the author, the work was valued.
2
Identify the second commentary
Al-Jawahir Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri is also anonymous, author died before 879 AH. Only the first part survives, in the Library of Congress.
The manuscript evidence gives us a tangible link to the past.
3
Note the evidence of their use
Ibn al-Humam quotes Fawa'id, and 'Allamah Qasim quotes Al-Jawahir in his Tashih al-Quduri. This proves the commentaries were consulted by later authorities.
Citations are key to establishing a work's influence.
4
Recognize the importance of anonymous works
Anonymous commentaries are common in Islamic scholarship; they represent the collective effort of the madhhab. Their preservation and citation indicate their acceptance as authoritative sources.
Do not dismiss a work simply because the author is unknown.

Commentary
The text from the third chapter of a work listing commentaries on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar introduces two anonymous commentaries. First, Fawa'id al-Quduri, cited by Kamal ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir. Second, Al-Jawahir Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri, of which only the first part survives in the Library of Congress. The author notes that 'Allamah Qasim ibn Qutlubugha quotes from Al-Jawahir in his Tashih al-Quduri. These entries provide bibliographic information about early Hanafi commentaries.

Key Terms
فوائد القدوري (Fawa'id al-Quduri): An anonymous commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, cited by Ibn al-Humam.
الجواهر شرح مختصر القدوري (Al-Jawahir Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri): An anonymous commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, partially extant.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Tashih al-Quduri: Identifying the Mufta Bihi Positions
Introduction to a tashih work

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the purpose of Tashih al-Quduri and explain its methodology for identifying the mufta bihi positions.

Core Idea
Tashih al-Quduri distills the operative Hanafi fatwa from ikhtilaf.

The Hanafi school, like others, contains multiple transmitted opinions on many issues. Later scholars compiled works to clarify which opinion is to be relied upon (mufta bihi) in practice. One such work is Tashih al-Quduri by Qasim ibn Qutlubugha. This lesson introduces the work's purpose and methodology as explained in its preface.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and his intent
Read the passage to determine who wrote Tashih al-Quduri and why. The text begins: '٦٧- تصحيح القدوري... وهو للإمام قاسم بن قطلوبغا... ألفه لبيان القول المفتى به في المذهب'.
The author is Qasim ibn Qutlubugha (d. 879 AH). His intent is to clarify the mufta bihi opinion.
2
Understand the term 'mufta bihi'
The phrase 'القول المفتى به' means the opinion that is issued as fatwa. In the Hanafi school, this is the preponderant (rajih) view after weighing evidence and scholarly authority.
Contrast with 'marjuh' (weak) opinions that are not acted upon.
3
Analyze the methodology
Qasim ibn Qutlubugha says he will write corrections (tasḥīḥ) on memorized mukhtasar texts, attributing each correction to its source, similar to the Shafi'i practice. He will mention where earlier scholars like Burhan al-Shari'ah and al-Nasafi have agreed.
This shows a rigorous source-attribution method, not mere personal preference.
4
Note reliance on earlier authorities
He cites Imam Burhan al-Shari'ah al-Mahbubi and Imam Abu al-Barakat al-Nasafi as having claimed to include the most correct or relied-upon opinions. This indicates a cumulative tradition of tashih.
These earlier works are themselves references for the mufta bihi.
5
Recognize the addition from Qadi Khan
He includes corrections from the Fatawa of Qadi Khan, whom he regards as highly reliable. This shows that later fatwa collections also inform the mufta bihi hierarchy.
Qadi Khan's fatawa are a major source for Hanafi tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦٧- تصحيح القدوري أو الترجيح والتصحيح على القدوري). و هو للإمام قاسم بن قطلوبغا، المتوفى سنة ٨٧٩هـ ، رحمه الله تعالى. وقد ألفه لبيان القول المفتى به في المذهب، المصحح المرجح . من أقوال أئمة المذهب حال اختلافهم، وذلك بنقل أقوال علماء المذهب في ذلك من مصادر كثيرة. وقد جاء في مقدمته بعد كلام طويل عن لزوم اتباع الراجح دون المرجوح، قال: ... ولما تم لي هذا النظر، أحببت أن أضع على المختصرات التي تحفظ في هذا الزمان تصحيحات معزوة إلى قائلها، أو ناقلها، كما فعله الأئمة الشافعية لمختصراتهم، وإن كان ذلك موجوداً في الشروح والمطولات، إلا أنهم أسعفوا بذلك مـن لـم يصل إلى تلك. وقد قال الإمام برهان الشريعة المحبوبي في أول كتابه: إنه حاو لما هو أصح الأقاويل والاختيارات. وقال الإمام أبو البركات النسفي في صدر كتابه : وأورد في هـذا الكتاب ما هو المعول عليه في الباب. فأذكر في المسائل المعروفة أنهما قد اعتمدا ذلك، وربما ذكرتُ مَن وافَقَهما على ذلك. وهذا ما تيسر على مختصر القدوري رحمه الله ، مع زيادات نص على تصحيحها القاضي الإمام فخر الدين قاضيخان في فتاواه، فإنـه من أحق من يُعتمد على تصحيحه والله ولي الإعانة، وهـو حـسـبي ونعم الوكيل». اهـ
67 - Tashih al-Quduri, or al-Tarjih wa al-Tashih 'ala al-Quduri. It is by Imam Qasim ibn Qutlubugha, died 879 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. He authored it to clarify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) statement in the school, the corrected and preferred [opinion] from the statements of the imams of the school when they differ, by transmitting the statements of the scholars of the school on that from many sources. In his introduction, after a long discussion on the necessity of following the preponderant (rajih) over the preponderated (marjuh), he said: '... When this consideration was completed for me, I liked to write corrections (tashih) on the mukhtasarat (abbreviated texts) that are memorized in this time, attributing them to their sayer or transmitter, as the Shafi'i imams did for their mukhtasarat, even though that is present in the commentaries and lengthy works, but they facilitated that for those who do not reach those [volumes]. Imam Burhan al-Shari'ah al-Mahbubi said at the beginning of his book: it (this work) contains the most correct sayings and choices. And Imam Abu al-Barakat al-Nasafi said in the preface of his book: I present in this book what is relied upon in the chapter. So I mention in the well-known issues that both of them have relied upon that, and perhaps I mention who agreed with them on that. This is what was facilitated on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, may Allah have mercy on him, with additions whose correction was stated by the judge Imam Fakhr al-Din Qadi Khan in his Fatawa, for he is among the most deserving of being relied upon in his correction. And Allah is the grantor of assistance, and He is sufficient for me and the best disposer of affairs.' End.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage introduces the work Tashih al-Quduri by Qasim ibn Qutlubugha (d. 879 AH). The author's purpose is to identify the mufta bihi (operative) opinion in the Hanafi school when there is disagreement. In his preface, he explains his methodology: he will write corrections (tashih) on the popular mukhtasar texts, attributing each correction to its source, similar to the practice of Shafi'i scholars. He notes that earlier Hanafi scholars like Burhan al-Shari'ah al-Mahbubi and Abu al-Barakat al-Nasafi already claimed to include the most correct or relied-upon opinions in their works. Qasim ibn Qutlubugha will mention where those scholars have agreed on a position, and he will also add corrections from the Fatawa of Qadi Khan, whom he considers highly reliable.

Key Terms
تصحيح القدوري (Tashih al-Quduri): A work by Qasim ibn Qutlubugha that determines the correct (mufta bihi) Hanafi position on issues in Mukhtasar al-Quduri.
القول المفتى به (al-qawl al-mufta bihi): The opinion that is issued as fatwa; the relied-upon ruling in the school.
الراجح (al-rajih): The preponderant opinion after evaluation of evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of Tashih al-Quduri according to its preface?
A) To write a completely new mukhtasar
B) To identify the mufta bihi (operative) opinions in cases of disagreement
C) To criticize the Shafi'i school
D) To summarize all opinions without preference

Key Takeaway
Tashih al-Quduri is a crucial reference for determining the operative Hanafi ruling when there are multiple transmitted opinions.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Hanafi Method of Tashih: Attributing Corrections to Their Sources
Author explains his method of attributing corrections to sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi concept of tashih and identify the key principle that requires a mufti to know the source of a ruling.

Core Idea
A Hanafi mufti must know the source of each ruling; tashih attributes corrections to the original authority.

This passage introduces the author's methodology in his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. He explains why he felt compelled to produce a work that attributes each correction to its speaker or transmitter, following the practice of Shafi'i scholars. The passage also highlights a key principle from Imam Abu Hanifa that prohibits giving fatwa without knowing the source of the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's purpose
The author states he wanted to provide corrections (tashihat) to the memorized abridgments, attributing each correction to its original speaker (qa'il) or transmitter (naqil). Understand what 'tashih' means in this context.
Tashih is about verification and attribution, not editorial correction.
2
Compare with the Shafi'i method
The author notes that Shafi'i scholars already did this for their abridgments. Recognize that the Hanafi school needed a similar systematic attribution.
3
Analyze the quotations from earlier Hanafi authorities
Imam Burhan al-Shari'ah al-Mahbubi claimed his work contains 'the most correct of sayings and choices.' Imam Abu al-Barakat al-Nasafi stated he includes 'what is relied upon.' The author will mention when both agreed.
4
Connect to Abu Hanifa's principle
The narration from Abu Hanifa through Abu Yusuf says: 'It is not permissible for anyone to give a fatwa according to our statement unless he knows from where we said it.' This is the core motivation for the author's work.
5
Recognize the later influence
Tashih al-Quduri was widely relied upon by later Hanafi scholars and incorporated into al-Lubab. The passage ends with notes on two printed editions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث : شروح مختصر القدوري
من أقوال أئمة المذهب حال اختلافهم، وذلك بنقل أقوال علماء المذهب في ذلك من مصادر كثيرة.
وقد جاء في مقدمته بعد كلام طويل عن لزوم اتباع الراجح دون المرجوح، قال:
... ولما تم لي هذا النظر، أحببت أن أضع على المختصرات التي تحفظ في هذا الزمان تصحيحات معزوة إلى قائلها، أو ناقلها، كما فعله الأئمة الشافعية لمختصراتهم، وإن كان ذلك موجوداً في الشروح والمطولات، إلا أنهم أسعفوا بذلك من لم يصل إلى تلك.
وقد قال الإمام برهان الشريعة المحبوبي في أول كتابه: إنه حاو لما هو أصح الأقاويل والاختيارات.
وقال الإمام أبو البركات النسفي في صدر كتابه : وأورد في هذا الكتاب ما هو المعول عليه في الباب.
فأذكر في المسائل المعروفة أنهما قد اعتمدا ذلك، وربما ذكرت من وافقهما على ذلك.
وهذا ما تيسر على مختصر القدوري رحمه الله، مع زيادات نص على تصحيحها القاضي الإمام فخر الدين قاضيخان في فتاواه، فإنه من أحق من يُعتمد على تصحيحه والله ولي الإعانة، وهو حسبي ونعم الوكيل». اهـ

وقد قال المؤلف في كتابه تاج التراجم، في ترجمة إبراهيم بن يوسف، حيث ورد فيه ذكر رواية أبي يوسف أن الإمام أبا حنيفة رحمه الله قال: «لا يحل لأحدٍ أن يُفتي بقولنا ما لم يعرف من أين قلناه»، فعلق العلامة قاسم على ذلك بقوله:
«قلت: وهذه الرواية هي التي حملتني على شرحي للقدوري، الذي ذكرت فيه من أين أخذوا علمهم». اهـ

* وقد اعتمد كتاب تصحيح القدوري كل من جاء بعده من أئمة المذهب، ونقلوا عنه، بل ضمنه العلامة الشيخ عبد الغني الغنيمي الميداني في كتابه اللباب في شرح الكتاب، كما صرح في مقدمته. وقد طبع الكتاب بتحقيق الأخ الفاضل الدكتور عبد الله نذير، ووضع في أعلى الصفحات نص مختصر القدوري لربط تصحيح ٦٧٠ صفحة، به، وقد جاء الكتاب في مجلد كبير في القدوري وكانت الطبعة الأولى في مؤسسة الريان ببيروت، سنة ١٤٢٦هـ. وقد وقع في النص المحقق ملاحظات كثيرة، تدعو إلى إعادة النظر فيه، ومراجعة الأصول الخطية لتصحيحها، والكمال الله وحده. كما طبع الكتاب في بيروت في دار الكتب العلمية، ط ١٤٢٣/١هـ، بتحقيق الأستاذ ضياء يونس، الذي نال بتحقيقه درجة الماجستير، وهذه الطبعة أصلح نصاً من سابقتها.
Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. From the sayings of the imams of the madhhab when they disagreed, by transmitting the statements of the scholars of the madhhab from many sources. And it came in his introduction after lengthy speech about the necessity of following the preferred (rajih) over the rejected (marjuh), he said: ... When this consideration was complete for me, I loved to place upon the abridgments that are memorized in this time corrections (tashihat) attributed to their speaker or transmitter, as the Shafi'i imams did for their abridgments, even though that exists in the commentaries and lengthy works, but they aided with that one who did not reach those. And Imam Burhan al-Shari'ah al-Mahbubi said at the beginning of his book: It contains what is the most correct of sayings and choices. And Imam Abu al-Barakat al-Nasafi said in the preface of his book: I mention in this book what is relied upon in the chapter. So I mention in the well-known issues that they both relied on that, and perhaps I mention who agreed with them on that. And this is what was facilitated for Mukhtasar al-Quduri, may Allah have mercy on him, with additions that the Qadi Imam Fakhr al-Din Qadikhan confirmed in his Fatawa, for he is among the most deserving to be relied upon for his correction, and Allah is the Granter of success, and He is sufficient for me, and the best disposer of affairs. End quote. And the author said in his book Taj al-Tarajim, in the biography of Ibrahim ibn Yusuf, where mention of the narration of Abu Yusuf occurs that Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'It is not permissible for anyone to give a fatwa according to our statement as long as he does not know from where we said it.' So the scholar Qasim commented on that saying: 'I said: This narration is what drove me to write my commentary on al-Quduri, in which I mentioned from where they took their knowledge.' End quote. And the book Tashih al-Quduri was relied upon by everyone who came after him from the imams of the madhhab, and they transmitted from it. Rather, the scholar Sheikh Abd al-Ghani al-Ghanimi al-Maydani included it in his book al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab, as he stated in his introduction. And the book was printed with the investigation of the virtuous brother Dr. Abdullah Nadhir, and he placed at the top of the pages the text of Mukhtasar al-Quduri to link the correction of 670 pages to it, and the book came in one large volume. The first edition was at Mu'assasat al-Rayyan in Beirut, year 1426 AH. And there occurred in the verified text many observations that call for reconsideration and reviewing the manuscript sources to correct them, and perfection belongs to Allah alone. Also the book was printed in Beirut at Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyah, edition 1423/1 AH, with the investigation of Ustadh Diya Yunus, who obtained a master's degree for his investigation, and this edition has a more correct text than the previous one.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is from the introductory chapter of the book, discussing the methodology of the commentary and the importance of attributing corrections to their sources. The author explains that after thorough consideration, he decided to provide corrections (تصحيحات) to the memorized abridgments (المختصرات) of his time, attributing each correction to its original speaker or transmitter, following the practice of the Shafi'i scholars. He notes that such corrections already exist in longer commentaries but are not accessible to everyone. He then quotes two earlier Hanafi authorities: Imam Burhan al-Shari'ah al-Mahbubi, who claimed his work contains the most correct opinions and choices, and Imam Abu al-Barakat al-Nasafi, who stated he includes in his book what is relied upon (المعول عليه) in each chapter. The author states that for well-known issues, he will mention that both these imams relied on that view, and sometimes he will also mention who agreed with them. He further notes that he has made additions to the Mukhtasar al-Quduri that are confirmed by Qadi Fakhr al-Din Qadikhan in his Fatawa, emphasizing that Qadikhan's corrections are highly reliable. The passage then quotes a narration from Abu Hanifa through Abu Yusuf: 'It is not permissible for anyone to give a fatwa according to our statement unless he knows from where we said it.' This is said to have motivated the author to write his commentary, showing the source of the madhhab's rulings. Finally, the passage mentions the reliance on Tashih al-Quduri by later scholars, including its inclusion in al-Lubab by Abd al-Ghani al-Ghanimi al-Maydani, and discusses the printed editions, noting that the second edition (Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah, 1423 AH) is textually superior to the first.

Key Terms
تصحيح (tashih): Correction of a legal text, attributing the correct ruling to its source (qa'il or naqil).
مرجوح (marjuh): The weaker or less preferred opinion within the madhhab, which should not be followed.
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A famous Hanafi fiqh text by Imam Abu al-Husayn al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), used as a foundational matn for memorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what motivated the author to write his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri?
A) To refute the Shafi'i school's method
B) The narration from Abu Hanifa about knowing the source
C) A request from Qadi Fakhr al-Din Qadikhan
D) The need to shorten the text

Key Takeaway
Attribution of rulings to their sources is essential for proper tashih in the Hanafi school.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Mufti Must Know the Source: A Foundational Hanafi Principle
Introduction to commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri and the principle of knowing sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition Imam Abu Hanifa placed on issuing fatwa and explain why knowledge of source evidence is essential in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A mufti may not issue a ruling without knowing its proof from the sources.

This passage introduces the third chapter of a work on Hanafi jurisprudence, focusing on the commentaries of Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It begins with a powerful narration from Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) that sets a non-negotiable condition for anyone who wishes to give fatwa according to the Hanafi school: one must know the source evidence from which the ruling is derived. This principle drove later scholars like Qasim ibn Qutlubugha to write extensive commentaries that traced each ruling back to its primary proofs, thereby preserving the school's commitment to evidence-based jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Locate the narration from Imam Abu Hanifa in the passage. Read it carefully and state the condition he imposes on giving fatwa. This is the central legal point of the entire section.
The Arabic text reads: 'لا يحل لأحد أن يفتي بقولنا ما لم يعرف من أين قلناه.'
2
Understand the motivation
Notice how Qasim ibn Qutlubugha explicitly states that this narration compelled him to write his commentary 'Tas-hih al-Quduri'. Reflect on why knowing the source is so critical in Hanafi methodology.
Qasim's goal was to show 'from where they took their knowledge,' thereby fulfilling the Imam's condition.
3
Recognize the transmission of knowledge
The passage details how later Hanafi scholars relied on Qasim's work, and even incorporates it into later commentaries like al-Lubab. It also describes a manuscript written in the author's lifetime with an ijaza, illustrating the care in preserving authentic knowledge.
This step emphasizes the communal effort to uphold the principle: the chain of transmission ensures each fatwa is grounded in its source.

Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
And the author said in his book Taj al-Tarajim, in the biography of Ibrahim ibn Yusuf, where the narration of Abu Yusuf is mentioned that Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'It is not permissible for anyone to give a fatwa with our statement unless he knows from where we said it.' Then the scholar Qasim commented on that by saying:
'I say: This narration is what compelled me to write my commentary on al-Quduri, in which I mentioned from where they took their knowledge.' End quote.
* And the book Tas-hih al-Quduri was relied upon by all who came after him from the imams of the madhhab, and they transmitted from it; rather, the scholar Sheikh Abdul Ghani al-Ghunaimi al-Maidani included it in his book al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab, as he stated in its introduction. The book was printed under the editorship of the brother Dr. Abdullah Nadhir, and he placed at the top of the pages the text of Mukhtasar al-Quduri to link with it, and the book came in a large volume of 670 pages [for Tas-hih al-Quduri]. The first edition was at Mu'assasat al-Rayyan in Beirut, in 1426 AH. There were many observations in the edited text that call for reconsideration and review of the manuscript sources to correct them, and perfection belongs to Allah alone. The book was also printed in Beirut at Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah, edition 1423/1 AH, edited by Professor Diya' Yunus, who obtained his Master's degree for his editorship, and this edition is more correct in text than the previous one.

The manuscript copies of the book are many; see al-Fihris al-Shamil and the introduction of the editor Diya'. Among the best copies is the copy of al-Maktabah al-Ahmadiyyah in the city of Halab al-Shahba', which settled in Maktabat al-Asad in Damascus, under number (13879), written in 866 AH during the author's lifetime, in the handwriting of one of his students, Abu al-Khayr Muhammad Zain al-Din ibn Shams al-Din Muhammad ibn Imran al-Hanafi, resident of Jerusalem, and the author authorized him with it at the end of this copy, and mentioned in his authorization to him: that he read this book to him with a reading of mastering its meanings and certainty of its significations. And with me, praise to Allah, of the book and its two editions are images of four manuscript copies.

Al-Muhibbi mentioned this commentary in his biography in Khulasat al-Athar (2).

Commentary
This passage is a frontmatter introduction to the third chapter of a Hanafi fiqh work, discussing commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The author begins by quoting a famous narration from Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) that one should not issue a fatwa without knowing the source evidence. This underscores the importance of understanding the methodology and proofs behind the rulings of the madhhab. The scholar Qasim ibn Qutlubugha (d. 879 AH) comments that this narration motivated him to write his commentary 'Tas-hih al-Quduri', where he clarifies the sources of the rulings. The passage then notes that this book was widely relied upon by later Hanafi scholars, and was even incorporated by al-Maidani in his 'al-Lubab'. It provides bibliographic details about printed editions and manuscript copies, including a valuable copy written during the author's lifetime with an ijaza (authorization) to his student. Finally, it lists two more commentaries: one by Ibrahim ibn Biri Zada and another by Ibn Shabana, with brief biographical notes. This passage serves as a historical and bibliographic orientation, emphasizing that authentic fatwa requires knowledge of the source evidence, a principle central to Hanafi methodology.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A concise Hanafi fiqh manual by Abu al-Husayn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), used as a foundational text in Hanafi curriculum.
تصحيح القدوري (Tas-hih al-Quduri): A commentary and correction on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by the scholar Qasim ibn Qutlubugha, focusing on verifying the text and explaining the sources of rulings.
إجازة (Ijaza): An authorization given by the author to a student to transmit the book, as mentioned in the context of the manuscript copy.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti must know the source evidence (dalil) for a ruling before issuing a fatwa; this principle is central to the school's methodology.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
The Transmission of Hanafi Knowledge: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
List of commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the purpose and value of sharh literature in the Hanafi tradition, and trace the scholarly chain of transmission from al-Quduri to later commentators using the provided bibliography.

Core Idea
Commentaries (shuruh) preserve the chain of ijazah and the living tradition of Hanafi fiqh.

This passage from Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri is not a legal ruling but a scholarly bibliography. It lists several commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, one of the foundational texts of the Hanafi school. Understanding such lists helps us appreciate how knowledge was transmitted and which works were valued. You will learn to read these entries as maps of scholarly authority and connection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text and its significance
Recognize that Mukhtasar al-Quduri is a core matn (text) in Hanafi fiqh. It was authored by Imam Ahmad al-Quduri (d. 428 AH). The commentaries listed are all expansions on this matn.
The base text is always the starting point for understanding the chain of transmission.
2
Note the manuscript details and ijazah
The author highlights a copy written in 866 AH during al-Quduri's lifetime by his student, with an ijazah (authorization). This demonstrates the importance of direct teacher-student transmission.
Ijazah is a key concept in Islamic education, ensuring authenticity.
3
List the commentators and their biographical data
Each entry includes the commentator's name, teachers, notable positions, death date, and sometimes sources where the commentary is mentioned. Compile this information for each.
Biographical details help assess the commentator's authority and the reliability of the commentary.
4
Understand the role of these works in the Hanafi curriculum
These commentaries served as teaching tools, elaborating on the matn with proofs and clarifications. They represent different layers of scholarly engagement (e.g., tashih, shamil).
Some commentaries, like that of Ibrahim Biri Zadeh (Mufti of Mecca), held greater authority.
5
Trace the scholarly chain (sanad) from al-Quduri to later scholars
Use the teacher-student relationships mentioned (e.g., Ibn Shabanah studied under Qasim ibn Qutlubugha) to build a chain of transmission. This connects the commentators back to the founder of the school.
The sanad is a hallmark of Islamic learning; it validates the knowledge.

Chapter Three: Commentaries of Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The manuscript copies of the book are many; see the comprehensive index and see the introduction of the investigation of Professor Diya'. Among its best copies: the copy of the Ahmad Library in the city of Halab al-Shahba', which settled in the Library of al-Asad in Damascus, under number (13879), written in the year 866 AH, during the author's lifetime, in the handwriting of one of his students, namely Abu al-Khayr Muhammad Zayn al-Din ibn Shams al-Din Muhammad ibn 'Imran al-Hanafi, the resident of noble Jerusalem, and the author authorized him with it at the end of this copy, and mentioned in his authorization to him: that he read this book to him with a reading of mastery of its structures and certainty of its meanings. I have from the book, praise be to God, along with its two editions, images of four manuscript copies.
68- Sharh Tashih al-Quduri (the correction by 'Allamah Qasim). By 'Allamah Sheikh Ibrahim ibn Husayn ibn Biri Zadah, Mufti of Makkah al-Mukarramah, born in Madinah al-Munawwarah in 1023 AH, died in Makkah al-Mukarramah in 1099 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Al-Muhibbi mentioned this commentary in his biography in Khulasat al-Athar.
69- Al-Shamil Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri. By the distinguished jurist Shams al-Din Abu al-Lutf Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Ali al-Husayni al-Khatib al-Misri, famous as Ibn Shabanah, one of the students of 'Allamah Qasim ibn Qutlubugha, died in 919 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Ibn Tulun mentioned this book and his biography in "Mut'at al-Adhhan min al-Tamattu' bi al-Iqran bayna Tarajim al-Shuyukh wa al-Aqran". And from him, 'Abd Allah al-Habashi mentioned it in Jami' al-Shuruh wa al-Hawashi.
70- Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri. By 'Allamah Muhammad Shah ibn Muhammad known as Ibn al-Hajj Hasan al-Rumi al-Mudarris, author of numerous works, died in 939 AH, may Allah have mercy on him.
71- Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri. By Nasir ibn al-Husayn (al-Hasan) ibn Mahma al-'Alawi al-Busti, died after 940 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Thus in Kashf al-Zunun, and his death year is not mentioned.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a biographical/bibliographical section from Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri, listing several commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The author first notes the abundance of manuscripts and highlights a particularly valuable copy written during the author's lifetime by his student, Abu al-Khayr, with the author's personal authorization (ijazah). He then lists four specific commentaries: (68) Sharh Tashih al-Quduri by Ibrahim ibn Husayn Biri Zadeh, who was Mufti of Mecca; (69) Al-Shamil Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Shams al-Din Ibn Shabanah, a student of Qasim ibn Qutlubugha; (70) another commentary by Muhammad Shah ibn al-Hajj Hasan al-Rumi; and (71) one by Nasir al-Busti. For each, he provides the author's name, brief biography, and sometimes notes where the work is mentioned in other biographical dictionaries. This section serves to establish the chain of transmission and scholarly engagement with al-Quduri's work.

Key Terms
شرح (sharh): A commentary or explanation of a base text, in this case, of Mukhtasar al-Quduri.
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A famous concise manual of Hanafi fiqh by Imam Ahmad al-Quduri, used as a core text in madrasas.
إجازة (ijazah): A license or authorization from a scholar to transmit a text or teach, often written at the end of a manuscript.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The passage illustrates how Hanafi scholars preserved and transmitted their legal tradition through commentaries (shuruh) that carried the chain of ijazah.

Three Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri: Biographical Overview
Biographical listing of three Hanafi commentators on Mukhtasar al-Quduri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three Hanafi commentators on Mukhtasar al-Quduri and state their key biographical details.

Core Idea
Mukhtasar al-Quduri attracted commentaries from Hanafi scholars across centuries and regions, from Anatolia to al-Andalus.

This lesson introduces three commentaries on the foundational Hanafi text Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The passage from Al-Lubab Sharh al-Quduri lists these works, highlighting their authors, death dates, and sources. By examining these biographies, you will appreciate the wide geographical and temporal range of Hanafi scholarship. Understanding the chain of commentators helps in evaluating the authority and perspective of different sharh works.

Guided Path
1
Identify each commentator and his death date
Scan the passage for the names and death years: Muhammad Shah bin Muhammad (Ibn al-Hajj Hasan) died 939 AH, Nasir bin al-Husayn al-Busti died after 940 AH, and 'Abd al-Awwal bin Husayn died 950 AH.
Death years are given in the Islamic calendar (AH).
2
Note the geographical origins
Identify where each scholar was active: Ibn al-Hajj Hasan was an Ottoman scholar from Anatolia (Rumi), al-Busti was from Bust (likely in present-day Afghanistan) and resided in Cordoba, and 'Abd al-Awwal's origin is not specified but likely in the broader Hanafi heartlands.
Geographical spread shows the global reach of Hanafi scholarship.
3
Locate the sources cited for each biography
The passage references multiple biographical sources: Kashf al-Zunun, Taj al-Tarajim, Hadiyyat al-'Arifin, and others. Note how different editions of Taj al-Tarajim differ in including al-Busti's biography.
These sources help verify the reliability of the information.
4
Examine the structure of the commentary
Only 'Hadq al-'Uyun' is described as a 'mixed commentary' (مختصر ممزوج), interweaving the original matn with explanation. The others are likely separate commentaries.
This affects how one reads and references the commentary.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these commentaries were written and how they contribute to the Hanafi tradition. They demonstrate the ongoing need for explanation and the vitality of the madhhab.
Each commentary adds a layer of interpretation and practical guidance.

Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a biographical listing from a modern edition of Al-Lubab Sharh al-Quduri. It is part of the third chapter (الفصل الثالث) which catalogs the numerous commentaries written on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Imam al-Quduri. The section presents three entries numbered 70, 71, and 72. Entry 70 describes a commentary by the Ottoman scholar Muhammad Shah bin Muhammad (d. 939 AH), known as Ibn al-Hajj Hasan, who was a teacher and author. Entry 71 covers a commentary by Nasir bin al-Husayn (or Hasan) al-Busti (d. after 940 AH), a Hanafi scholar who resided in Cordoba (modern Spain). The text notes that his death year is left blank in some sources, and a different edition of Taj al-Tarajim omits his biography. Entry 72 introduces 'Hadq al-'Uyun' (literally 'The Pupils of the Eyes'), a mixed commentary (مختصر ممزوج, meaning the commentary text interweaves with the original matn) by the judge 'Abd al-Awwal bin Husayn (d. 950 AH), who lived nearly a hundred years. The work begins with 'al-Hamdu lillah...'. The passage demonstrates the rich tradition of Hanafi scholarship and the wide geographical range of its commentators, from Anatolia to al-Andalus. Understanding such biographical details helps students appreciate the chain of transmission and authority of different commentaries within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
شرح مختصر القدوري (Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri): Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, a renowned Hanafi fiqh manual by Imam al-Quduri.
تاج التراجم (Taj al-Tarajim): A biographical dictionary by Qasim bin Qutlubugha (d. 879 AH), used as a source for scholars' biographies.
حدق العيون (Hadq al-'Uyun): Title of a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri; 'hadq' means the pupil of the eye, and 'al-'uyun' means eyes, so 'Pupils of the Eyes'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Commentator of Mukhtasar al-Quduri and the Virtue of Fiqh
Biographical and prefatory material about a commentator on Mukhtasar al-Quduri.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preponderant name of the commentator of Mukhtasar al-Quduri and describe how his preface elevates the status of fiqh as the revealer of Islam's reality.

Core Idea
The commentator's name is Abd al-Awwal, and his preface proclaims fiqh as the science that unveils the essence of Islam.

This lesson introduces the biographical background of a commentator on Mukhtasar al-Quduri and the opening of his preface. While no legal rulings are derived here, understanding the author and his preface sets the stage for appreciating his commentary's approach to Hanafi fiqh. The passage resolves a confusion about the commentator's name and highlights the lofty status of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commentator and the name dispute
Read the passage to find that two names appear: 'Abdullah' in the manuscript introduction and 'Abd al-Awwal' in other sources. Note that the editor leans toward 'Abd al-Awwal'.
The manuscript itself uses 'Abdullah', but external evidence favours 'Abd al-Awwal'.
2
Analyze the evidence for the correct name
List the sources cited: al-Shaqa'iq, Kashf al-Zunun, Hadiyat al-'Arifin. Observe that they consistently use 'Abd al-Awwal' and mention his other works.
The commentator's death date (950 AH) and age also support the identification.
3
Examine the preface's praise of fiqh
Read the preface excerpt: it calls fiqh the science that reveals the reality of Islam and informs about legal rulings. Note the traditional opening (basmalah, hamdalah, salawat).
This reflects the high esteem for fiqh in Hanafi scholarship.
4
Connect the preface to the commentary's purpose
Realize that the commentator wrote this work to explain Mukhtasar al-Quduri because of the book's nobility and conciseness. The preface sets the tone for a serious fiqh exposition.
Understanding the author's intent helps when using the commentary.

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and front-matter introduction from the modern editor's preface of a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It begins by identifying the third chapter of the book, which covers commentaries on al-Quduri's work. The text discusses the identity of a commentator whose work is being introduced: there is a discrepancy between the name 'Abdullah' appearing in some manuscripts and 'Abd al-Awwal' as given by Sezgin and other sources. The passage provides evidence that the correct name is 'Abd al-Awwal', citing references from al-Shaqa'iq and the author's age. It then transitions to the commentator's own preface, which opens with a traditional basmalah, hamdalah, and salawat, praising Allah and the Prophet Muhammad. The preface emphasizes the importance of fiqh as the science that reveals the reality of Islam and explains legal rulings. It notes that many works have been written on fiqh, and among them, Mukhtasar al-Quduri is particularly noble and concise. The passage cuts off before completing the sentence. The content is purely introductory and does not contain any legal rulings or proofs, serving to orient the reader to the commentary and its author.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A concise Hanafi fiqh manual by Imam al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), widely studied in the Hanafi madhhab.
عبد الأول (Abd al-Awwal): The preponderant name of the commentator of Mukhtasar al-Quduri, an Ottoman-era Hanafi scholar (d. 950 AH).

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Author's Introduction to Lubab: Purpose and Textual Variant
Author's preface explaining motive, dedication, and a textual variant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's motive for writing the commentary and identify the significance of the plural vs. singular form of 'taharah' in the manuscript tradition.

Core Idea
A commentary's introduction reveals its aim, patronage, and textual considerations — not just fiqh rulings.

This passage is the author's preface to *Lubab*, his commentary on al-Quduri’s *Mukhtasar*. It explains why he wrote the work — to provide a concise yet clear explanation for students — and dedicates it to Sultan Bayazid II. He also addresses a minor textual variant: the title ‘Kitab al-Taharat’ appears in both plural and singular forms across manuscripts. This discussion, while not a legal ruling, highlights the care Hanafi scholars took in their textual traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and his work
Who wrote this commentary? What is its title? The author is ‘Abdullah ibn Husayn al-Maidani, and the commentary is named 'Hadq al-‘Uyun' (The Gaze of Eyes).
The title is a play on words: 'Lubab' means core, and 'Hadq al-‘Uyun' means the gaze of eyes.
2
Analyze the motive
What prompted the author to write this commentary? Read his statement: he wanted to produce a work between excessive length and deficient brevity, making it clear and easy to memorize for students.
This is a common goal in Hanafi sharh literature: balancing clarity and conciseness.
3
Locate the dedication
To whom is the work dedicated? The author praises Sultan Bayazid II (Abu al-Fath Bayazid Muhammad al-Ghazi) and credits him with reviving the study of fiqh in schools.
The sultan's support indicates the close relationship between Ottoman rulers and Hanafi scholars.
4
Examine the textual variant
What does the author say about the title 'Kitab al-Taharat'? Some manuscripts have it in the plural (al-Ṭaharāt) and some in the singular (al-Ṭaharah). The author explains both: the plural reflects different types of purification; the singular as a masdar covers all types generically.
This is a grammatical discussion, not a juristic one. Both forms are acceptable.
5
Assess the significance
Does this variant affect the fiqh rulings within the book? No, it is purely a textual consideration. Understanding this prevents misinterpreting a manuscript difference as a legal disagreement.
Hanafi scholars often note such variants to ensure accuracy of transmission.

بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم، وبه نستعين على القوم الكافرين، الحمد الله على عواطف كرمه وسوابغ نعمه، والصلاة على بشير رحمته، ونذير نقمته، محمد أمين وحيه، وخاتم رسله، الذي أرسله وأعلام الهدى دارسة، ومناهج الدين طامسة، فرفع الحق وأشاد، وقمع الباطل وأباد، وأظهر الإسلام، وأفاد، وشرع الأحكام كما أراد، وعلى آله وأصحابه الذين أزاحوا من الأرض الفساد.

وهو الباذل نفسه الشريفة لإحياء الأحكام الشرعية، حيث أمر في المدارس بدرس الكتب الفقهية بعد ما كانت الهمم قاصرة، والرغبات فاترة، حرسه الله من عل الليالي والأيام، ما تعاقبت الشهور والأعام، ورضي عن آبائه الكرام، وأجداده العظام، رضوان الله تعالى عليهم إلى يوم الحشر والقيام.
وأنا العبد الضعيف الراجي عفو ربه الكريم الماجد عبد الله بن حسین بن حسن بن حامد، عفا عنهم الواحد. وقع في أكثر النسخ: (كتاب الطهارات) : بلفظ الجمع، وفي بعض النسخ بلفظ الواحد. وجه الأول: اختلاف أنواع الطهارة حداً وحقيقة، ووجه الثاني : كون الطهارة مصدراً لكونه الحقيقة المشتركة، جميع الأنواع والأفراد، فلا حاجة إلى الجمع. وهي واحدة، فيشمل
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, and with Him we seek help against the disbelieving people. Praise be to Allah for the gifts of His generosity and the abundance of His blessings. And prayers be upon the bearer of His mercy, the warner of His punishment, Muhammad, the trustee of His revelation, and the seal of His messengers, whom He sent when the signs of guidance were obliterated and the paths of religion were effaced. So he raised the truth and exalted it, suppressed falsehood and destroyed it, manifested Islam and benefited, and legislated the rulings as He willed. And upon his family and companions who removed corruption from the earth. To proceed: Indeed, the knowledge of fiqh – its grandeur and high standing are not hidden, for it is the revealer of the reality of Islam and the discloser of the rulings of the laws. Numerous books and splendid volumes have been written on it. Since the Mukhtasar of al-Quduri among them is a noble book and a concise, elegant work, I have written a commentary on it, between excessive length and deficient brevity, making it clear for those who desire it and easy to memorize for students. I did this only for beautiful remembrance and abundant reward, and I named it 'Hadq al-‘Uyun' (The Gaze of Eyes). This was during the reign of the greatest sultan, master of the necks of nations, the victorious king, the sword of the world and faith, sultan of Islam and Muslims, leader of kings and sultans, conqueror of the disbelievers and heretics, Abu al-Fath Bayazid Muhammad al-Ghazi in the path of Allah for exalting the word of Allah – may Allah support him with His mighty victory over his enemies, and by His manifest conquest break the appearance of his opponents. He devoted his noble person to reviving the Shariah rulings, ordering in the schools the study of books of fiqh after the aspirations had become inadequate and desires weak. May Allah protect him from the afflictions of nights and days, as long as months and years follow one another, and be pleased with his noble fathers and great ancestors – may Allah's pleasure be upon them until the Day of Resurrection and Judgment. I, the weak slave hoping for the forgiveness of his Noble, Glorious Lord, ‘Abdullah ibn Husayn ibn Hasan ibn Hamid – may the One forgive them. In most copies it occurs: 'Kitab al-Taharat' (Book of Purifications) in the plural form, and in some copies in the singular form. The reason for the first: the different types of taharah in definition and reality. The reason for the second: that taharah is an infinitive, being the common reality, encompassing all types and individuals, so there is no need for plural. And it is one, so it includes.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This is a front-matter passage from Sharh al-Quduri by ‘Abdullah ibn Husayn al-Maidani, where he introduces his commentary named 'Hadq al-‘Uyun'. He begins with the basmala and praises Allah and the Prophet, then explains the importance of fiqh and his motivation for writing this commentary – to make the Mukhtasar of al-Quduri accessible for students without being too verbose or too brief. He dedicates the work to Sultan Bayazid II (Abu al-Fath Bayazid Muhammad al-Ghazi), who actively supported the study of fiqh in schools. The author then mentions his own name and lineage. Finally, he addresses a textual variant: the title of the first section is 'Kitab al-Taharat' – some manuscripts have it in the plural (al-Taharat, meaning types of purification) while others have it in the singular (al-Taharah, as the common reality). The singular form suffices because taharah as a masdar covers all forms. This is a stylistic/grammatical consideration, not a substantive fiqh discussion.

Key Terms
الطهارات (al-ṭaharāt): Plural form of taharah, used in the title to refer to the different types and categories of purification (e.g., wudu, ghusl, tayammum).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) To earn the sultan's favor
B) To make fiqh easier for students without being too long or too short
C) To correct errors in previous commentaries
D) To promote the Hanbali school

Key Takeaway
The author's introduction reveals his motive (accessibility), patronage (Sultan Bayazid II), and attention to textual precision (plural vs. singular form of 'taharah').

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Textual Variants in Hanafi Manuscripts: The Case of 'Kitab al-Taharat'
Biographical preface and manuscript note

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reason for the plural and singular textual variant in the title of Mukhtasar al-Quduri and assess the importance of manuscript transmission in preserving Hanafi fiqh texts.

Core Idea
The plural 'al-taharat' highlights multiple types of purity, while the singular 'al-taharah' is a viable collective noun.

This passage from the introduction of the commentary 'Hadq al-'Uyun' on Mukhtasar al-Quduri offers a glimpse into the scholarly concern for textual accuracy. The author notes that the first book is titled 'Kitab al-Taharat' (plural) in most copies, but 'Kitab al-Taharah' (singular) in others, and provides a linguistic justification for both. Understanding this variant is not just a philological curiosity—it teaches us how Hanafi jurists approached their foundational texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Author and His Purpose
The author is Abdullah bin Husayn bin Hasan bin Hamid, who wrote the commentary 'Hadq al-'Uyun' as a balanced exposition. He states he wrote it for reward and to facilitate memorization.
2
Recognize the Role of the Sultan
He composed it under Sultan Bayezid II, who revived the study of fiqh in schools. This highlights the political support for Hanafi scholarship.
The sultan's patronage helped preserve and propagate Hanafi texts.
3
Analyze the Textual Variant
In most manuscripts, the title is plural; in some, singular. Understand that this is a philological issue, not a legal one.
The variant does not affect the rulings themselves, only the title.
4
Understand the Linguistic Reasoning
The plural form reflects the diversity of types of purity (wudu, ghusl, tayammum). The singular form treats taharah as a verbal noun encompassing all types.
Both are valid from a linguistic standpoint.
5
Consider Manuscript Transmission
The editor provides information on surviving manuscript copies of 'Hadq al-'Uyun', indicating the text's availability and provenance.
This is critical for verifying textual accuracy.

(1) والكتاب مخطوط بعد، ومنه عدة نسخ، ينظـر لـهـا الفهـرس الشامل، وتوجد منه نسخة في المكتبة المركزية بجامعة الملك عبد العزيز بجدة. كما توجد منه نسخة في جامعة الإمام محمد بن سعود بالرياض، مصوّرة من نسخة مخطوطة بروضة خيري، بالقاهرة بمصر، برقم (۸۹)، وتقع في (۲۲۹) ورقة، وفي كل صفحة (٢١) سطراً. وفي آخرها: «مَلَكَ هذا الكتاب بعون الملك الوهاب: إبراهيم بــن علي الحنفي، بتاريخ الثلاث والألف الهجرية...». اهـ وقد حصلت والله الحمد على صورة منها عن صورة لدى الأخ الفاضل الأستاذ محمد إسحاق، من جنوب إفريقية، جزاه الله خيراً. وهو شرح متوسط، ومما يُسعى إلى خدمته وإخراجه. ۷۳- شرح مختصر القدوري . للحلبي إبراهيم بن محمد بن إبراهيم، المتوفى سنة ٩٥٦ هـ، رحمه الله تعالى. .VAE/T (1)

Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is a prefatory note from the edition of the book اللباب شرح القدوري. It begins by quoting the introduction of another commentary, حدق العيون, written by عبد الله بن حسين بن حسن بن حامد. The author explains that he named his commentary 'حدق العيون' (The Eyes' Pupils) as a concise yet comprehensive work. He composed it during the reign of Sultan Bayezid II (the Ottoman Sultan), who revived the study of fiqh in schools. The author also notes a textual variant in the title 'كتاب الطهارات': some manuscripts have it in the plural (الطهارات) while others have the singular (الطهارة). He gives reasons for each: the plural because there are different types of taharah (purity), and the singular because the word is a verbal noun that covers all types. The quotation ends by mentioning that the book begins with taharah because salah is the pillar of religion. Then the current editor (or cataloguer) provides manuscript information: the location of copies of حدق العيون, its folio count, and a note about a manuscript owned by Ibrahim al-Hanafi. The passage also lists another commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by al-Halabi Ibrahim bin Muhammad bin Ibrahim (d. 956 AH). This is a standard scholarly catalogue entry, not a fiqh ruling.

Key Terms
الطهارات (al-taharat): The plural form used in the title of the first book of Mukhtasar al-Quduri, referring to various types of purity (wudu, ghusl, tayammum).
شرح (sharh): A commentary that explains and expands upon a base text (matn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because salah, which requires taharah, is the pillar of religion
C) Because the sultan commanded it
D) Because most students struggle with taharah

Key Takeaway
The textual variant in the title of Mukhtasar al-Quduri's first chapter is a linguistic nuance, not a legal dispute, illustrating the care Hanafi scholars take with manuscript transmission.

Introduction and Front Matter

Key Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
Bibliographic listing of commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three named commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri and explain the methodological features of Multaqa al-Abhar, especially its use of the dual form and preference indicators.

Core Idea
The Hanafi textual tradition is built on a core text and later commentaries that clarify, consolidate, and rank positions.

This passage provides an editor's overview of key Hanafi commentaries on al-Quduri's primer. It highlights three specific works and details the methodology of Multaqa al-Abhar, a compilation that merged four major texts. Understanding these layers is essential for navigating Hanafi fiqh literature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text
Mukhtasar al-Quduri is the foundational text that all these commentaries explain or compile. Recognize its role as the common source.
This text is one of the primary canonical works of the Hanafi school.
2
List the three named commentaries
The passage mentions: (1) Hadq al-Uyun (an unnamed commentary at the start), (2) Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri by al-Halabi (item 73), and (3) Multaqa al-Abhar by al-Halabi (item 74).
The first two are straightforward explanations; the third is a compilation.
3
Distinguish types of commentary
Hadq al-Uyun and Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri are explanatory (sharh), while Multaqa al-Abhar is a compilation that combines multiple texts.
Multaqa includes Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, Kanz al-Daqa'iq, and al-Wiqaya.
4
Analyze the author's methodology in Multaqa
Al-Halabi explicitly states he presents the strongest opinions, uses 'qil' or 'qalu' with 'al-asahh' to indicate weaker views, and uses the dual form (tathniyah) without context to refer exclusively to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn).
This methodology allows the reader to quickly identify preferred positions and attributions.
5
Relate to the Hanafi tradition of tarjih
The practice of preferring stronger opinions (tarjih) is central to later Hanafi works. Multaqa exemplifies this by explicitly ranking views.
Understanding this helps in using later fatwa compilations correctly.

It is a medium-length commentary, and it is among those worthy of being served and published. 73- Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri by al-Halabi Ibrahim bin Muhammad bin Ibrahim, died 956 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. .VAE/T (1) 464 Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri And it has several copies, see the comprehensive index, among which is a copy in the public endowments in Mosul, Husayn Bey, comprising 129 folios. 74- Multaqa al-Abhar (compiled between Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar al-Mawsili, Kanz al-Daqa'iq al-Nasafi, and al-Wiqaya of Sadr al-Shari'ah). By al-Halabi Ibrahim bin Muhammad bin Ibrahim, died 956 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. In his introduction: 'Praise be to Allah who guided us to understanding the religion... Afterward: Some seekers of benefit asked me to compile a book including the issues of al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Kanz, and al-Wiqaya in an easy, uncomplicated expression, so I responded to that, and added to it some needed issues from al-Majma', and a bit from al-Hidayah, and I explicitly mentioned the differences among our Imams, presenting their strongest opinions and delaying others, unless I qualified it with what indicates preference. As for the disagreements among later scholars or between the mentioned books, everything I begin with 'qil' or 'qalu' if accompanied by 'al-asahh' etc., it is weaker compared to what is not. Whenever I mention the dual form without a clue to its referent, it refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.' .ε7+/0 (1)
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographic section from the editor's introduction to 'Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri', detailing the third chapter on commentaries of Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It first mentions an existing commentary called 'Hadq al-Uyun', which begins with a statement about prayer. The editor notes that this commentary is still in manuscript and provides locations of copies, including a manuscript in Jeddah and a photocopy in Riyadh. He also mentions that a copy was obtained from a scholar in South Africa. The editor describes this commentary as 'medium-length' and worthy of publication. Next, the passage lists two commentaries by al-Halabi Ibrahim bin Muhammad bin Ibrahim (d. 956 AH): the first is simply 'Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri', with a copy in Mosul consisting of 129 folios. The second is 'Multaqa al-Abhar', which brings together four major Hanafi texts: Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar lil-Fatwa, Kanz al-Daqa'iq, and al-Wiqaya. The author's introduction explains his methodology: he presents the texts in simple language, adds supplementary issues from al-Majma' and al-Hidayah, clarifies differences between Hanafi imams, and gives preference to stronger opinions. When he uses 'qil' or 'qalu' with 'al-asahh', it indicates a weaker view. The dual form ('huma') without context always refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). This passage is a valuable resource for understanding the Hanafi textual tradition.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A foundational Hanafi fiqh text by Imam al-Quduri, forming the base for many commentaries.
ملتقى الأبحر (Multaqa al-Abhar): A Hanafi fiqh compilation by al-Halabi that combines four major texts: Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, Kanz al-Daqa'iq, and al-Wiqaya.
التثنية (al-tathniyah): The dual form in Arabic grammar, used by al-Halabi to refer to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn).

Understanding Hanafi Commentaries: The Case of Fātih al-Qudūrī
Describing the features and sources of a Hanafi commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features of a Hanafi commentary (sharh) by analyzing the description of Fātih al-Qudūrī, and evaluate its scholarly methodology.

Core Idea
A Hanafi commentary explains the matn, justifies arrangement, provides proofs, addresses objections, and engages ikhtilaf.

This passage introduces us to a mid-length commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It is not a legal ruling but a bibliographic description of a scholarly work. Understanding how such commentaries are structured helps us navigate the vast Hanafi literary tradition. In this lesson, we will extract the characteristic features of a Hanafi sharh from this passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of work
Determine that the passage describes a commentary (sharh) on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Note its title 'Fātih al-Qudūrī' and the fact that the author is unknown.
Not all commentaries are attributed; some are anonymous.
2
Extract the features mentioned
List all the features explicitly mentioned: explanation of chapter order, rational and textual proofs, objections (fa-in qulta/qultu), intra- and inter-madhhab ikhtilaf, and citation of sources.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يهتم كثيراً بذكر المناسبات'.
3
Assess additional characteristics
Note the inclusion of Turkish phrases, indicating the author's origin, and the list of sources (al-Hidayah, al-Nafi', etc.). Consider how these enhance the commentary's utility.
The presence of Turkish suggests a regional audience.

Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri (1) It was mentioned by Sezgin in the History of Arabic Heritage, as well as by the authors of the Comprehensive Index, and they listed three copies for it: the first in the India Office in London, consisting of 227 folios, written in the 12th century. A second copy in Lahore, 198 folios, and a third copy in the Egyptian National Library in Cairo. I came across a photocopy of a copy from Punjab at the Islamic University of Madinah, number 1568, film 2/?, consisting of 171 folios. It is a copy missing its introduction; despite extensive study I could not ascertain the author's name. I note that this copy has a patchwork introduction, where on its first page a picture of the first page of a lithograph edition of Mukhtasar al-Quduri is placed, and before it about two pages in Urdu were written, whose translation indicates that the book 'Fātih al-Qudūrī' is a commentary on al-Quduri, that its author was around the 10th century AH, and that it was written during the reign of the Sultan of India (Dar Darai), whose rule was from 1160 AH to 1182 AH. (1) 122/3 (2) 3/7 And from my study of this commentary, I saw it as a mid-length commentary, very useful, and it pays great attention to mentioning the connections in the arrangement of al-Quduri's books and chapters, and it mentions the reason for putting this before that and delaying this after that, and one benefits greatly from this commentary in this aspect. It also pays attention to rational and textual proof for fiqh issues, and it presents objections with 'if you say', then answers them with 'I say'. It frequently mentions the disagreement among the imams of the madhhab, and often mentions the disagreement of al-Shafi'i, and sometimes others. As for its sources, those explicitly cited include: al-Hidayah by al-Marghinani, al-Nafi' by al-Samarqandi, al-Mughrib by al-Mutarrizi, al-Sihah by al-Jawhari, al-Musaffa by al-Nasafi, and others. What indicates that its author is a Turkish Roman is that he includes passages in Turkish from the commentary, for example in the Book of Divorce he says: (i.e., the husband's statement to the woman 'you are divorced' means in Turkish 'sen yoš olijigí stek'). end quote, and the like. -77 Sharh Dibajat al-Quduri (Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri) by Muhammad ibn 'Abdillah al-Ilbasani, one of the scholars of the 10th century AH, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and bibliographic description of a commentary on Mukhtar al-Quduri known as 'فاتح القدوري' (Fātih al-Qudūrī). The author, whose name is not preserved in the available manuscript, is identified as a Turkish scholar (Rumi) from around the 10th-11th century AH. The commentary is described as mid-length (متوسط) and very useful. It pays special attention to explaining the order of chapters and books in Mukhtasar al-Quduri, giving reasons for priorities. It also provides textual (naqli) and rational ('aqli) proofs for legal rulings, presents objections with 'فإن قلت' and answers with 'قلت', frequently mentions differences among Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), and often cites Imam al-Shafi'i's views. The author explicitly references sources such as al-Hidayah by al-Marghinani, al-Nafi' by al-Samarqandi, al-Mughrib by al-Mutarrizi, al-Sihah by al-Jawhari, and al-Musaffa by al-Nasafi. The presence of Turkish phrases (e.g., in the chapter on divorce 'سن يوش أوليجي ستك' meaning 'you are divorced') confirms his Turkish origin. The passage ends with a brief mention of another commentary: 'شرح ديباجة القدوري' (Sharh Dibajat al-Quduri) by Muhammad ibn 'Abdillah al-Ilbasani, also from the 10th century AH.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): The well-known Hanafi fiqh primer by Imam al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), widely studied in the Hanafi curriculum.
فاتح القدوري (Fātih al-Qudūrī): The title of the commentary being described in this passage, meaning 'The Opener of al-Quduri'.
الهداية (al-Hidayah): A major Hanafi legal reference by al-Marghinani (d. 593 AH), a commentary on Bidayat al-Mubtadi'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The passage you are about to read comes from 'Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri' and introduces two notable commentaries on the foundational Hanafi text Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Commentary
This passage from 'Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri' presents two commentaries on the foundational Hanafi text 'Mukhtasar al-Quduri' by Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri (d. 428 AH). The first commentary is by Muhammad ibn Abdallah al-Ilyabani, who dedicated his work to the Ottoman Sultan Murad III (r. 982-1004 AH). The manuscript copy mentioned is a royal presentation copy written in beautiful naskh script. The second commentary is by Abd al-Rahim ibn Ali al-Amadi al-Diyarbakri, a scholar from the 10th century AH. In his introduction, al-Amadi states his aim to write a comprehensive yet concise commentary covering the four Sunni schools of law, incorporating insights from major Hanafi works such as 'Sadr al-Shari'ah', 'Al-Nuqayah', 'Al-Zayla'i', and 'Al-Hidayah'.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A foundational text in Hanafi fiqh by Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), widely studied and commented upon.
شرح (Sharh): A commentary that explains and expands upon a base text (matn).
الهداية (Al-Hidayah): A famous Hanafi fiqh commentary by Burhan al-Din al-Marghinani (d. 593 AH), which is itself a commentary on a commentary.
الإلياباني (al-Ilyabani): Muhammad ibn Abdallah al-Ilyabani, author of a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri dedicated to Sultan Murad III.
العمادي الدياربكري (al-Amadi al-Diyarbakri): Abd al-Rahim ibn Ali al-Amadi al-Diyarbakri, a 10th-century AH scholar who wrote a comprehensive commentary covering the four Sunni schools.

Author's Methodology and Sources in Al-Muhimm al-Daruri
Author's preface explaining his commentary's methodology and sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key sources and methodological principles that 'Abd al-Rahim al-Amidi used in his commentary Al-Muhimm al-Daruri.

Core Idea
Al-Muhimm al-Daruri synthesizes earlier authoritative works into a concise yet comprehensive Hanafi commentary.

This lesson explores the author's preface to his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Understanding the author's methodology and sources helps us appreciate how later Hanafi scholars built upon the foundational texts. The author explains his motivation, his approach to balancing conciseness with completeness, and the key works he relies on.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the commentary
Read the author's statement of intent: why did he write this sharh? What problem does it solve?
He mentions the widespread use of al-Quduri's Mukhtasar and the need for a commentary that includes proof and reasoning without verbosity.
2
List the major sources cited
The author explicitly names the works he draws from. Identify each and note its significance in the Hanafi school.
Sadr al-Shari'ah's al-Nuqayah, al-Zayla'i's Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, and al-Hidayah by al-Marghinani.
3
Analyze the author's methodological approach
What does he claim about the content? Look for keywords like 'proof', 'reasoning', 'principles', 'fatwas', 'derivation'.
He promises to include proofs (dalil) and legal reasoning (ta'lil), along with universal principles (qawa'id kulliya) and verified fatwas.
4
Understand the title and its significance
What does the title 'Al-Muhimm al-Daruri' mean, and how does the author describe his work?
He calls it 'the greatest sea in knowledge' and says it 'encompasses the commentaries of al-Hidayah', indicating comprehensiveness.
5
Note the historical context
The author includes a chronogram and mentions the ruling Sultan. This provides a date and shows the work's connection to the Ottoman era.
The chronogram yields 945 AH, and the manuscript copy is from 970 AH during Sultan Sulayman's reign.

469
By Judge 'Abd al-Rahim ibn 'Ali al-Amidi (1) al-Diyarbakri, died in the tenth century AH, may Allah have mercy on him.
The author said in his introduction:
...
Now then: The poor before the Rich Allah, 'Abd al-Rahim al-Amidi ibn 'Ali, may Allah beautify him and his parents and continue His blessings upon them and him, says: Indeed, the book of the Shaykh, the Imam, the All-Knowing, the Most Learned, the Verifier, the Proficient, the Sagacious, Abu al-Husayn al-Baghdadi Ahmad al-Quduri, may Allah cover him with His forgiveness and lodge him in His highest Paradise, since its use has become frequent, it has gained an ample share of acceptance, and the motives to care for it in memorization and understanding have abounded, I have rolled up the sleeves of earnestness with resolve and zeal to write a commentary on it, a commentary in which I will unfold what it contains of principles and rulings, encompassing proof and legal reasoning, free of verbiage and elongation. I have added to it compendious points from the unique doctrines of the four eminent Imams, benefits that the mouths of inkpots and tongues of pens have praised, summaries of researches with words that are few yet indicative, and virgin ideas adorned with the benefits of pearls.
Combining all that is in Sadr al-Shari'ah, al-Nuqayah, and the verifications of al-Zayla'i and al-Hidayah.
(1) Kashf al-Zunun 2/1634, Hadiyyat al-'Arifin 1/562; see also Mu'jam al-Makhtutat al-Mawjudah fi Maktabat Istanbul wa-Anatoli 2/788 (2400).
[PAGE_470]
470
Chapter Three: Commentaries on al-Quduri
Opening every book and chapter with what befits it of fundamental principles and high maxims, and clarifying its issues with the jewels of fatwas that have sought their difficulties from the shining stars, and universal principles to which the jurist may refer, and a consolidation of rulings upon which the mufti may rely, along with what I have added to it from authentic texts that delight the eye, and from verification of proofs and derivation firm in both aspects.
This, and the tongue is too short for lengthy praise; Allah knows the corrupter from the reformer, and to Him is the return.
I have named it: "Al-Muhimm al-Daruri 'ala Kitab al-Quduri," and it is the greatest sea in knowledge (dirayah), encompassing commentaries of al-Hidayah.
So its date became with the crescent of Qaws: "O Guide, grant the Garden of Paradise" (1), for the Imam of our time, and the Sultan of our era, the Sultan, the Sultan, the Sultan...
...
ibn Sulayman Khan ibn Sultan Salim ibn Sultan Bayezid... may mercy and forgiveness be upon them.
Thus ends the author's introduction.
And on the margin of this copy, the owner of the Jarallah library wrote that it is a copy written in the year 970 AH during the lifetime of Sultan Sulayman al-Uthmani.
(1) By this sentence the date according to abjad calculation becomes the year 945 AH, which is the time of the mentioned Sultan Sulayman's reign.
It is not clear to me the reason for the author naming the abjad date as: 'Crescent of Qaws.' (2) This introduction was copied from the manuscript of Jarallah copy number (730) in Istanbul, and was written to me by brother Professor Muhammad Fateh Qaya, may Allah reward him well.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface to his commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The author, 'Abd al-Rahim al-Amidi, explains his motivation: al-Quduri's work is widely used and accepted, so he felt compelled to write a commentary that is comprehensive yet concise. He aims to include principles (qawa'id), rulings (ahkam), proofs (dalil), and reasoning (ta'lil) without verbosity. He also incorporates the positions of the four great Imams (the Hanafi school primarily, but also the others), benefiting from earlier works like Sadr al-Shari'ah's al-Nuqayah, al-Zayla'i's Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, and al-Hidayah by al-Marghinani. He describes his commentary as opening each section with appropriate foundational principles, clarifying issues with well-verified fatwas, and providing universal rules for jurists and muftis. He names his work 'Al-Muhimm al-Daruri 'ala Kitab al-Quduri,' calling it the greatest ocean of knowledge, encompassing the commentaries of al-Hidayah. He concludes with a chronogram indicating the year 945 AH. The passage ends with a note about the manuscript copy written in 970 AH during Sultan Sulayman's reign.

Key Terms
شرح (sharh): A commentary or exposition of a base text (matn), here on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar.
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A seminal Hanafi fiqh manual by Ahmad al-Quduri, widely studied in the madhhab.
تصدير الشريعة (Sadr al-Shari'ah): A reference to the Hanafi scholar 'Ubayd Allah al-Mahbubi (d. 747 AH), author of al-Nuqayah and other works.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which major Hanafi works does he draw from for his commentary?
A) Sadr al-Shari'ah, al-Nuqayah, al-Zayla'i, al-Hidayah
B) Al-Mughni, al-Muhalla, al-Umm
C) Only al-Quduri's Mukhtasar
D) Fath al-Qadir, al-Bahr al-Ra'iq

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi's preface reveals his aim to produce a commentary that is comprehensive, concise, and grounded in the most authoritative Hanafi sources, making it a valuable bridge between the matn and the larger tradition.

Introduction and Front Matter

Introduction to al-Muhimm al-Daruri: Historical Context and Purpose
Introducing a major commentary on al-Quduri and its historical background

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the title, author's purpose, and the Ottoman sultan for whom the commentary 'al-Muhimm al-Daruri' was written, and correct a common historical error.

Core Idea
Al-Muhimm al-Daruri is a comprehensive Hanafi commentary written for Sultan Suleiman the Lawgiver.

This passage comes from the introduction to a modern edition of a commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. It explains the author's methodology and dedicates the work to Sultan Suleiman I, correcting an earlier misattribution to Sultan Selim I. Understanding this context helps you appreciate the scholarly lineage and authority of Hanafi commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the work and its author
Note the title 'al-Muhimm al-Daruri 'ala Kitab al-Quduri' and understand it is a commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, a foundational Hanafi text.
The author describes it as comprehensive, opening each chapter with principles and rules.
2
Understand the author's methodology
The author clarifies issues using 'jewels of fatwas' and general rules for jurists and muftis, indicating a practical, fatwa-oriented approach.
He also includes sound hadiths and verification of evidence.
3
Decode the dating method
The date is given using abjad (numerical value of letters) via the phrase 'crescent of Sagittarius' which yields 945 AH (1538-39 CE).
The editor notes the abjad calculation but is unsure why the author used that specific zodiac phrase.
4
Identify the dedicatee
The work is dedicated to Sultan Suleiman Khan, son of Selim, not Selim I as mistakenly recorded in some sources.
The editor corrects an error in Fu'ad Sarakin's History of Arab Heritage.
5
Recognize the manuscript evidence
The Jar Allah library copy was written in 970 AH during Suleiman's lifetime, confirming the dedication.
Multiple copies exist; the Lale Li copy starts incomplete and led to the misattribution.

هذا، ولسان التقصير في طويل مدحه ،قصير، والله يعلم المفسد من المصلح، وإليه المصير.
