Chapter Three: Commentaries on al-Quduri. Opening every book and chapter with what befits it of principles and lofty rules, and clarifying its issues with jewels of fatwas whose problems have been examined by the luminous stars, and general rules to which the jurist refers, and a determination of rulings on which the mufti relies, along with what I included from sound hadiths that satisfy the eye, and from verification of evidence and deduction with mastery of both types. This, and the tongue of shortcoming in praising him at length is short, and Allah knows the corrupt from the reformer, and to Him is the return. And I named it: 'al-Muhimm al-Daruri 'ala Kitab al-Quduri', and it is the great ocean in knowledge, including the commentaries of al-Hidayah. So its date became with the crescent of Sagittarius: O Guide, grant the garden of Paradise, for the Imam of our time, and the Sultan of our era, the Sultan, the Sultan, the Sultan ... bin Suleiman Khan ibn Sultan Selim ibn Sultan Bayazid ... upon them mercy and forgiveness. End from the author's introduction. And it was written on the margin of this copy by the owner of the Jar Allah library that it is a copy written in the year 970 AH during the life of Sultan Suleiman the Ottoman. (1) By this sentence, the date according to the abjad system is the year 945 AH, which is the time of the rule of the mentioned Sultan Suleiman. And it is not clear to me the reason for the author naming the abjad date as 'the crescent of Sagittarius'. (2) This introduction was transmitted from the manuscript of the Jar Allah copy number 730 in Istanbul, and written to me by brother Professor Muhammad Fatih Qaya, may Allah reward him. I say: And Sultan Suleiman Khan ibn Sultan Selim, the tenth of the Ottoman Sultans, was pledged allegiance to the sultanate after the death of his father in the year 926 AH, and his term ended with his death in the year 974 AH, and his birth was in the year 900 AH, and he is called Sultan Suleiman the Lawgiver, because he established internal regulations for his government. (2) And there is an error in the History of Arab Heritage by Fu'ad Sarakin, for it states that he composed it for Sultan Selim I, who died in 926 AH, relying on the Lale Li copy (1016), which is an incomplete copy from its beginning, and begins with his words: '... Selim ibn Sultan Bayazid', and the correct is: Suleiman ibn Selim. And the book has several copies mentioned by the comprehensive index, and it appears to be a large commentary, as the first part of the Lale Li copy (1016) amounts to 346 folios, and the Jar Allah copy is in three parts. 79 - Ikhtisar Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Muhammad Badr al-Din al-Munshi, died 1001 AH, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a front matter section from a modern edition of a commentary on al-Quduri's work. It introduces the third chapter, which discusses various commentaries on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The author describes his own commentary, titled 'al-Muhimm al-Daruri 'ala Kitab al-Quduri', as a comprehensive work that opens each book and chapter with appropriate principles and rules, clarifies issues with valuable fatwas, and provides general rules for jurists and muftis. He gives the date of composition using the abjad system, yielding the year 945 AH (1538-39 CE), and dedicates the work to Sultan Suleiman the Lawgiver (r. 926-974 AH / 1520-1566 CE). The editor notes that the manuscript copy from the Jar Allah library was written in 970 AH during the sultan's lifetime. A correction is made regarding Fu'ad Sarakin's error in attributing the work to Sultan Selim I instead of Suleiman I. Finally, another work is listed: 'Ikhtisar Mukhtasar al-Quduri' by Muhammad Badr al-Din al-Munshi (d. 1001 AH). This passage serves to provide historical context for the commentary and clarify its dedication.

Key Terms
القدوري (al-Quduri): Reference to the author of Mukhtasar al-Quduri, a foundational Hanafi fiqh text.
المهم الضروري على كتاب القدوري (al-Muhimm al-Daruri 'ala Kitab al-Quduri): The title of the commentary being introduced, meaning 'The Important and Necessary on the Book of al-Quduri'.
هلال القوس (Hilal al-Qaws): Literal 'crescent of Sagittarius', used by the author to denote the abjad date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Al-Muhimm al-Daruri is a comprehensive Hanafi commentary dedicated to Sultan Suleiman I, composed in 945 AH, and its historical context is essential for correct attribution.

Manuscript Verification of Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
Bibliographic correction and commentary listing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correct bibliographic errors in manuscript attributions using internal evidence and cross-referencing.

Core Idea
Accurate attribution of texts relies on careful manuscript examination, not just secondary sources.

This passage from al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri corrects a common bibliographic error regarding the dedication of a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It also provides detailed notes on two later works: an abridgment and a commentary. As a student of Hanafi fiqh, understanding how manuscripts are verified helps you trace the transmission of knowledge and avoid relying on incorrect citations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the error being corrected
Find the mistake in Fu'ad Sarakin's History of Arab Heritage regarding the dedicatee of a commentary. The passage states the correct dedicatee is Sultan Suleiman the Lawgiver, not Sultan Selim I.
The error likely arose from an incomplete manuscript.
2
Examine the internal evidence provided
Note the manuscript details: Laleli 1016 is incomplete and starts with a truncated name. The author uses this to show how a partial reading led to misattribution.
Internal evidence includes the manuscript's condition (incomplete) and the beginning text.
3
Analyze the description of the first work (Ikhtisar)
Review Muhammad Badr al-Din al-Munshi's so-called abridgment. The passage clarifies it is actually a rearrangement, commentary, and supplement, not a mere abridgment.
This shows the importance of examining the actual content, not just the title.
4
Analyze the description of the second work (Sharh)
Note the details of Bustanzada's commentary: author name, title, death date, and source reference. Compare the level of detail given.
This entry is simpler, indicating perhaps less need for correction.
5
Synthesize the methodology
The author combines manuscript location, physical description (folios), completeness, and external correspondence (letter from Muhammad Fatih Qaya) to correct errors. This is a model for bibliographic research.
Always verify attributions by multiple manuscript copies and scholarly correspondence.

الفصل الثالث : شروح مختصر القدوري
قلت : والسلطان سلیمان خان ابن السلطان سليم عاشر سلاطين آل عثمان، بويع له بالسلطنة بعد وفاة والده سنة ٩٢٦هـ وانتهت مدته بموته سنة ٩٧٤ هـ، وكانت ولادته سنة ٩٠٠هـ، ويسمى بالسلطان سليمان القانوني، لما وضع من أنظمة داخلية لحكومته. وقد وقع خطأ في تاريخ التراث العربي (١) لفؤاد سركين، ففيه أنه ألفه للسلطان سليم الأول، المتوفى سنة ٩٢٦هـ، اعتماداً على نسخة لاله لي (١٠١٦)، وهي نسخة ناقصة من أولها، وتبدأ بقوله: «... سليم بن السلطان با يزيد والصواب : سليمان بن سليم. وللكتاب عدة نسخ ذكرها أصحاب الفهرس الشامل، ويظهر أنه شرح كبير، فقد جاء الجزء الأول من نسخة لاله لي (١٠١٦) بقدر (٣٤٦) ورقة، ونسخة جار الله في ثلاثة أجزاء.
٧٩- اختصار مختصر القدوري لمحمد بدر الدين المنشي، المتوفى سنة ١٠٠١ هـ، رحمه الله تعالى. ذكره سزكين في تاريخ التراث العربي، ومنه نسخة في لاله لي (١١٢٢)، في (١٠٨) ورقة، كتبت سنة ٩٩٣هـ. وقد اطلع على هذه النسخة الأخ الكريم الأستاذ محمد فاتح قايا، وكتب إلي من اسطنبول: إن النسخة ليست تامة، وآخرها هو كتاب الخنثى، وأن هذا الكتاب ليس باختصار لمختصر القدوري، بل هو ترتيب وتهذيب لفروعه، وشرح لبعض مسائله، واستدراك وتتميم لعمل المؤلف. وقد جاء في آخر الكتاب: تم وبالخير طم وع، ميّزه وأفرزه محمد بن بدر الدين المنشي، عامله الله بلطفه الخفي، في حادي عشر جمادى الآخرة، سنة تسع وسبعين وتسعمائة ٩٧٩هـ. نجز ذلك على يد أفقر العباد عبد المجيد المالكي، غفر الله ذنوبه، وقضى دَيْنَه ، في عشرين صفر، سنة ٩٩٣هـ. اهـ
٨٠- شرح مختصر القدوري لشيخ الإسلام قاضي العسكر محمد بن مصطفى العيشي التّيْرَوِي الرومي، المعروف بـ بستان زاده المتوفى باسطنبول سنة ١٠٠٦ رحمه الله تعالى. ذكره صاحب المخطوطات الموجودة باستانبول (١) ١٢٣/٣
Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. I say: Sultan Suleiman Khan, son of Sultan Selim, the tenth sultan of the Ottoman dynasty, was sworn allegiance as sultan after the death of his father in 926 AH, and his reign ended with his death in 974 AH. He was born in 900 AH, and he is called Sultan Suleiman the Lawgiver, because of the internal regulations he established for his government. And there is an error in the History of Arab Heritage by Fu'ad Sarakin, where it states that he wrote it for Sultan Selim I, who died in 926 AH, relying on the copy of Laleli (1016), which is an incomplete copy from its beginning, and it begins with his words: '... Selim ibn Sultan Bayezid,' when the correct is Suleiman ibn Selim. The book has several copies mentioned by the authors of the Comprehensive Index, and it appears that it is a large commentary, as the first part of the Laleli (1016) copy amounts to 346 folios, and the Jarullah copy is in three volumes. 79- Abridgment of Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Muhammad Badr al-Din al-Munshi, died in 1001 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Sezgin mentioned it in the History of Arab Heritage, and there is a copy in Laleli (1122), in 108 folios, written in 993 AH. The brother, Professor Muhammad Fatih Qaya, examined this copy and wrote to me from Istanbul: 'The copy is not complete, and its end is the Book of al-Khuntha (hermaphrodite), and that this book is not an abridgment of Mukhtasar al-Quduri, but rather an arrangement and refinement of its branches, and a commentary on some of its issues, and a supplement and completion of the author's work.' And at the end of the book it says: 'Completed and finished perfectly, distinguished and extracted by Muhammad ibn Badr al-Din al-Munshi, may Allah deal with him with His hidden kindness, on the eleventh of Jumada al-Akhirah, year 979. It was completed by the hands of the poorest of servants, 'Abd al-Majid al-Maliki, may Allah forgive his sins and pay his debt, on the twentieth of Safar, year 993.' End. 80- Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Shaykh al-Islam Qadi al-Askar Muhammad ibn Mustafa al-‘Ayshi al-Tirawi al-Rumi, known as Bustanzada, who died in Istanbul in 1006 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Mentioned by the author of Manuscripts Found in Istanbul (1) 123/3.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographic and biographical note from the author of al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri (likely al-Maydani). It begins with a correction of an error in Fu'ad Sarakin's History of Arab Heritage regarding the dedication of a commentary: it is dedicated to Sultan Suleiman the Lawgiver (Qanuni), not to his father Sultan Selim I. The author, using the phrase 'qultu' (I say), provides biographical details of Sultan Suleiman. He then lists two works related to Mukhtasar al-Quduri: first, an 'Ikhtisar' (abridgment) by Muhammad Badr al-Din al-Munshi, which is actually a rearrangement and commentary rather than a simple abridgment, as clarified by Muhammad Fatih Qaya. The second is a 'Sharh' (commentary) by Bustanzada (Muhammad ibn Mustafa al-‘Ayshi al-Rumi). The passage provides manuscript locations (Laleli and Jarullah) and states the pages/folios for each. The author follows the order: first the correction, then the two entries (79 and 80). This is typical of biographical dictionaries and bibliography sections in fiqh works, establishing the chain of transmission and scholarly lineage.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A concise Hanafi fiqh text by Imam al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), widely memorized and commentated upon.
اختصار (ikhtisar): An abridgment or summary of a longer work.
شرح (sharh): A commentary or explanation of a text.
نسخة (nuskhah): A manuscript copy of a work.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the basis for Fu'ad Sarakin's erroneous attribution of the commentary to Sultan Selim I?
A) He relied on a complete manuscript that clearly named Selim I.
B) He relied on an incomplete manuscript that started with '... Selim ibn Sultan Bayezid'.
C) He misread the colophon of a copy in Jerusalem.
D) He followed an earlier catalogue that contained the error.

Key Takeaway
Manuscript attribution must be verified through multiple copies and internal evidence, not solely on secondary sources.

Hanafi Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
Biographical list of Hanafi commentaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four Hanafi commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri and explain the value of the sharh tradition in Hanafi legal study.

Core Idea
Knowing the commentaries on a matn is essential for navigating Hanafi legal authority.

This passage from al-Lubab lists four commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, a core Hanafi text. Although it contains no rulings, understanding these commentaries helps you trace the development of Hanafi law and locate the most relied-upon positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and title of each commentary
Look for the author's full name, death date, and the title of the commentary. Note the sequence and any alternative names (e.g., al-Ashqar or al-Akhdab).
2
Note the author's relationship to the Hanafi school
Each author is a Hanafi scholar. Observe their death dates to see the chronological spread of commentaries.
3
Recognize the significance of the commentary tradition
Commentaries (shuruh) expand on concise matns, clarifying difficult passages, resolving disputes, and indicating the mufta bihi (operative) positions. This passage shows that al-Quduri's work attracted multiple commentaries over centuries.
4
Examine the textual confusion mentioned
Al-Maydani notes a discrepancy in Ibn Abidin's Hashiyah regarding the attribution of one commentary. This illustrates the importance of careful textual verification in Hanafi scholarship.
5
Relate to the present study of al-Lubab
Al-Maydani's al-Lubab is the commentary before you. Recognize that it stands in a tradition, drawing on earlier works like those of al-Ashqar and al-Istanbuli.

الفصل الثالث: شروح مختصر القدوري

للجمال الأشقر (أو للأخضب)، توفي قبل سنة ١٢٥٢هـ، رحمه الله تعالى.
ذكره ابن عابدين في حاشيته، في كتاب السرقة، قال: «لكن رأيتُ في شرح نظم الكنز ، للمقدسي، من كتاب الحجر قال : ونَقَل جد والدي لأمه : الجمال الأشقر في شرحه للقدوري : أن عدم جواز الأخذ .....». اهـ
قلت : لكن نَقَل ابن عابدين هذا النص بعينه في حاشيته في كتاب الحجر، وجعل شرح القدوري للأخضب، لا للجمال الأشقر، ونصه كما يلي: «تنبيه : قال الحموي في شرح الكنز، نقــلاً العلامة المقدسي عن جده الأشقر عن شرح القدوري للأخضب : أن عدم جواز الأخذ......... اهـ
وعليه فليحرر المؤلف، وذلك بالرجوع إلى شرح الكنز للحموي، أو شرح نظم الكنز للمقدسي، وهي مخطوطة، ولم أقف على ترجمة هذا، ولا هذا.

٩٩- شرح مختصر القدوري .
تأليف میر محمد بن محمد سعيد الاستانبولي، المعروف بطاهر سلام الرومي، المتوفى سنة ١٢٦٠هـ، رحمه الله تعالى.
ذكره البغدادي في هدية العارفين وإيضاح المكنون.

١٠٠ – اللباب في شرح الكتاب (شرح مختصر القدوري). للعلامة الشيخ عبد الغني بن طالب الغنيمي الميداني الدمشقي، المتوفى سنة ۱۲۹۸هـ ، رحمه الله تعالى وهو الكتاب الذي بين أيدينا.

١٠١ - شرح مختصر القدوري .
لمحمد المختار بن عثمان الباطومي، الملقب بمفتي زاده، كان حياً قبل سنة ۱۳١٢هـ، رحمه الله تعالى.
قال مؤلفه في مقدمته :
.... أما بعد : فيقول الفقير محمد المختار بن عثمان الباطومي، الملقب بمفتي زاده، أحسن الله له في الآخرة الحسنى وزيادة: هذه تعليقات رائقة وتقييدات فائقة على كتاب القدوري، الذي شاعت بركته كالعلم الضروري، وقد طار في الأقطار، وسار في الأمصار، وفاق في الاشتهار على الشمس في رابعة النهار، حتى أكب الناس عليه، وصار مفزعاً إليه. واقتنصتُ بشبكة الأفهام أجلَّ شوارده، وقيَّدتُ بأوتار الأقلام جُلَّ ...
Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri

By al-Jamal al-Ashqar (or al-Akhdab), died before the year 1252 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Ibn Abidin mentioned him in his Hashiyah, in the book of Theft, saying: 'But I saw in Sharh Nazm al-Kanz, by al-Maqdisi, from the book of Legal Restriction, he said: And my maternal grandfather quoted: al-Jamal al-Ashqar in his commentary on al-Quduri: that the impermissibility of taking...' I say: But Ibn Abidin quoted this very text in his Hashiyah in the book of Legal Restriction, and made the commentary on al-Quduri by al-Akhdab, not by al-Jamal al-Ashqar, and his text is as follows: 'Alert: al-Hamawi said in Sharh al-Kanz, quoting from al-Allamah al-Maqdisi from his grandfather al-Ashqar from the commentary of al-Akhdab on al-Quduri: that the impermissibility of taking...' Therefore, the author should investigate by referring to Sharh al-Kanz by al-Hamawi, or Sharh Nazm al-Kanz by al-Maqdisi, which are manuscripts, and I have not found a biography of this one or that one.

99- Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Authored by Mir Muhammad bin Muhammad Sa'id al-Istanbuli, known as Tahir Salam al-Rumi, died in the year 1260 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Al-Baghdadi mentioned him in Hadiyyat al-'Arifin and Idah al-Maknun.

100- al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab (Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri). By the eminent scholar Shaykh Abd al-Ghani bin Talib al-Ghunaimi al-Maydani al-Dimashqi, died in the year 1298 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. This is the book that we have before us.

101- Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. By Muhammad al-Mukhtar bin Uthman al-Batumi, known as Mufti Zada, was alive before the year 1312 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. The author said in his introduction: 'To proceed: The poor one, Muhammad al-Mukhtar bin Uthman al-Batumi, known as Mufti Zada, may Allah grant him goodness in the hereafter and more: These are excellent comments and superior annotations on the book of al-Quduri, whose blessing has spread like essential knowledge, and it has flown in regions, traveled in cities, and surpassed in fame the sun at midday, until people leaned towards it and made it their refuge. I grasped with the net of understanding its most elusive points, and tied with the strings of pens most of...'
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a biographical listing of four Hanafi commentaries on Imam al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, which is a foundational text in the Hanafi school. The author of al-Lubab, al-Maydani, is presenting the sources he used. The first commentary is attributed to al-Jamal al-Ashqar (or al-Akhdab), with a note about confusion in Ibn Abidin's references—a scholarly dispute over authorship. The second is by Mir Muhammad al-Istanbuli, with a death date of 1260 AH. The third is al-Lubab itself, by al-Maydani, identified as the work at hand. The fourth is by Muhammad al-Mukhtar al-Batumi (Mufti Zada), whose introduction praises al-Quduri's text. The passage demonstrates the chain of commentary tradition in the Hanafi school. Importantly, there is no fiqh ruling here; it is purely bibliographical for orientation.

Key Terms
شرح (sharh): A commentary or explanation of a foundational text (matn). Here it refers to expositions on Mukhtasar al-Quduri.
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): The concise legal manual by Abu al-Hasan Ahmad al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), a core Hanafi text.
اللباب (al-Lubab): The title of the commentary by al-Maydani, which is the current book under study.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) By Abd al-Ghani al-Maydani
D) By Muhammad al-Mukhtar al-Batumi

Key Takeaway
Knowing the chain of commentaries on a Hanafi matn is essential for correctly navigating the school's positions.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
The Role of Commentaries in Hanafi Fiqh: Mukhtasar al-Quduri
Lists two commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri with a quote from a preface and a scribal note

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the significance of Mukhtasar al-Quduri and identify two major commentaries on it, explaining why commentaries are essential for studying Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Commentaries unlock the concise matn, preserving and transmitting Hanafi fiqh across generations.

This passage from al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab opens a chapter listing commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It introduces two key works: al-Lubab itself and a commentary by Muhammad al-Mukhtar al-Batumi (Mufi Zadeh). The passage then quotes Mufi Zadeh's preface, which reveals the scholarly method of annotating and attributing rulings. Understanding the role of such commentaries is crucial for navigating Hanafi fiqh literature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text
Recognize that Mukhtasar al-Quduri is a concise manual of Hanafi fiqh by al-Quduri (d. 428 AH). It is the foundational matn being commented upon.
2
List the two commentaries mentioned
The passage names al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab by ‘Abd al-Ghani al-Maydani and Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Muhammad al-Mukhtar al-Batumi (Mufi Zadeh). Note their authors and death dates.
3
Analyze the purpose of commentaries
Mufi Zadeh's introduction explains that he wrote annotations (ta'liqat) to clarify rare points, attribute each branch to its root, and include proofs. This reflects the typical function of a sharh: expanding the matn with detail and evidence.
The phrase 'attributing every branch to its root' underscores the importance of tracing rulings back to primary sources.
4
Reflect on the scholarly chain
Consider how commentaries create a chain of transmission (isnad) for fiqh knowledge. Al-Lubab is the commentary before us, but the passage also notes earlier and later works, illustrating the living tradition.

The third chapter: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Al-Baghdadi mentioned it in Hadiyyat al-‘Arifin and Idah al-Maknun. 481. 100 – Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab (Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri). By the scholar Shaykh ‘Abd al-Ghani ibn Talib al-Ghanimi al-Maydani al-Dimashqi, died 1298 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. This is the book before us. 101 – Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri. By Muhammad al-Mukhtar ibn ‘Uthman al-Batumi, nicknamed Mufi Zadeh, alive before 1312 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Its author said in his introduction: ... To proceed: The poor one Muhammad al-Mukhtar ibn ‘Uthman al-Batumi, nicknamed Mufi Zadeh – may Allah grant him the best of the Hereafter and more – says: These are excellent annotations and superior notations on the book of al-Quduri, whose blessing spread like essential knowledge. It flew across regions and traveled in cities, surpassing the sun at its zenith in fame, until people flocked to it and sought refuge in it. I captured the noblest of its rare points with the net of understanding, and tied most of its marvels with the strings of pens. I began to adorn the margins of its delicate pages with what is truly the whiteness of the paper, attributing every branch to its root and everything to its place, including proofs and evidences and explanations of issues. Allah is sufficient for us, and He is the best disposer of affairs. There is no power or strength except through Allah, the Exalted, the Almighty. End. And written at the end of this copy: ‘Written by the pen of the one in need of the guidance of his Lord: Hasan Shawqi ibn ‘Uthman al-Wahbi al-Hazar Gharadi, who is engaged in teaching students at the Fatih Mosque in Constantinople by the grace of the King, the Bestower, in the year 1315 AH, in the 18th (month) of his birth, may prayers and peace be upon him.’ End. This name ‘Hasan Shawqi’ was written after a marginal note placed on the Hamdalah and prayer upon the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) that appears at the beginning of the copy of al-Quduri. He wrote on it: ‘This expression from the Basmalah to here is not found in the old copies, and its words indicate that it is not from the speech of the author, may Allah have mercy on him. But we wrote it as we found it, seeking blessing. Written by its editor Hasan Shawqi.’ End. Accordingly, the ‘editor’ refers to the one who prepared the book for printing, corrected it, and served it. And Allah knows best. The book was printed in an Ottoman edition, and the date of obtaining the printing license was 11 December of the year 1312? This is despite mentioning December? The printed copy is rare; one copy is deposited in the library of Shaykh…
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a front-matter section from al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab, a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by ‘Abd al-Ghani al-Maydani. It opens a chapter listing commentaries on al-Quduri’s work. Two main commentaries are noted: first, al-Lubab itself (the current book), and second, a commentary by Muhammad al-Mukhtar al-Batumi (Mufi Zadeh). The passage then quotes Mufi Zadeh’s introduction, which praises the original text’s widespread acceptance and explains his method of adding annotations while attributing everything to its sources. After the quotation, there is a note from a later scribe, Hasan Shawqi, who edited a manuscript. He clarifies that an initial part (from the Basmalah to a point) is not from the original author but was added for blessing. The passage concludes with bibliographic details about the Ottoman print. This is purely informational and does not contain legal rulings.

Key Terms
اللباب في شرح الكتاب (al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab): The title of the commentary authored by ‘Abd al-Ghani al-Maydani on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, meaning 'The Core in Explaining the Book'.
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A concise manual of Hanafi fiqh by Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), widely studied in the Hanafi madhhab.
مفتي زاده (Mufi Zadeh): Nickname of Muhammad al-Mukhtar al-Batumi, meaning 'son of the mufti', indicating his scholarly lineage.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
Textual Authenticity and Scribal Practices in Hanafi Manuscripts
Contains a scribe's note clarifying a textual addition at the beginning of Mukhtasar al-Quduri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a textual addition in a manuscript and explain the Hanafi scribe's approach to preserving authenticity while honouring received tradition.

Core Idea
Scribes distinguished authorial text from pious additions, preserving the chain of transmission.

This passage concludes with a crucial note from the scribe Hasan Shawqi, who identifies a section at the beginning of the manuscript (from the Basmalah to a prayer) as not belonging to the original author al-Quduri. Despite this, he includes the lines for blessing. This reveals the tension between textual authenticity and pious practice in Hanafi manuscript culture. Understanding such scribal norms is vital for anyone working with classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the scribe's note
Find the section beginning with 'وكتب في آخر هذه النسخة' and read the note by Hasan Shawqi. He identifies the added passage and explains why it was included.
2
Identify the added passage
The scribe specifies that the phrase from the Basmalah up to the prayer (صلوات) on the Prophet is not part of al-Quduri's original text.
The added passage is described as 'هذه العبارة من البسملة إلى هنا'.
3
Understand the scribe's reasoning
The scribe writes that the words indicate it is not from the author, but he wrote it 'as we found it, seeking blessing' (تبركاً). This shows respect for the received tradition while being transparent about its origin.
4
Reflect on the concept of tabarruk
Tabarruk (seeking blessing) sometimes motivated inclusion of non-authorial material. This is a common practice in Islamic manuscript culture.
5
Assess the impact on fiqh authority
Since the added lines are not from al-Quduri, they do not carry the same legal authority as the matn. A scholar must be aware of such additions to avoid attributing rulings incorrectly.

والكتاب مطبوع طبعة عثمانية، وتاريخ أخذ رخصة الطباعة في (١١) كانون الأول، سنة (۱۳۱۲) ، هكذا، مع أنه ذكـر شـهـر كـانون

والنسخة المطبوعة منه نادرة، منها نسخة مودعة في مكتبة الشيخ
The third chapter: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Al-Baghdadi mentioned it in Hadiyyat al-‘Arifin and Idah al-Maknun. 481. 100 – Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab (Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri). By the scholar Shaykh ‘Abd al-Ghani ibn Talib al-Ghanimi al-Maydani al-Dimashqi, died 1298 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. This is the book before us. 101 – Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri. By Muhammad al-Mukhtar ibn ‘Uthman al-Batumi, nicknamed Mufi Zadeh, alive before 1312 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Its author said in his introduction: ... To proceed: The poor one Muhammad al-Mukhtar ibn ‘Uthman al-Batumi, nicknamed Mufi Zadeh – may Allah grant him the best of the Hereafter and more – says: These are excellent annotations and superior notations on the book of al-Quduri, whose blessing spread like essential knowledge. It flew across regions and traveled in cities, surpassing the sun at its zenith in fame, until people flocked to it and sought refuge in it. I captured the noblest of its rare points with the net of understanding, and tied most of its marvels with the strings of pens. I began to adorn the margins of its delicate pages with what is truly the whiteness of the paper, attributing every branch to its root and everything to its place, including proofs and evidences and explanations of issues. Allah is sufficient for us, and He is the best disposer of affairs. There is no power or strength except through Allah, the Exalted, the Almighty. End. And written at the end of this copy: ‘Written by the pen of the one in need of the guidance of his Lord: Hasan Shawqi ibn ‘Uthman al-Wahbi al-Hazar Gharadi, who is engaged in teaching students at the Fatih Mosque in Constantinople by the grace of the King, the Bestower, in the year 1315 AH, in the 18th (month) of his birth, may prayers and peace be upon him.’ End. This name ‘Hasan Shawqi’ was written after a marginal note placed on the Hamdalah and prayer upon the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) that appears at the beginning of the copy of al-Quduri. He wrote on it: ‘This expression from the Basmalah to here is not found in the old copies, and its words indicate that it is not from the speech of the author, may Allah have mercy on him. But we wrote it as we found it, seeking blessing. Written by its editor Hasan Shawqi.’ End. Accordingly, the ‘editor’ refers to the one who prepared the book for printing, corrected it, and served it. And Allah knows best. The book was printed in an Ottoman edition, and the date of obtaining the printing license was 11 December of the year 1312? This is despite mentioning December? The printed copy is rare; one copy is deposited in the library of Shaykh…
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a front-matter section from al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab, a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by ‘Abd al-Ghani al-Maydani. It opens a chapter listing commentaries on al-Quduri’s work. Two main commentaries are noted: first, al-Lubab itself (the current book), and second, a commentary by Muhammad al-Mukhtar al-Batumi (Mufi Zadeh). The passage then quotes Mufi Zadeh’s introduction, which praises the original text’s widespread acceptance and explains his method of adding annotations while attributing everything to its sources. After the quotation, there is a note from a later scribe, Hasan Shawqi, who edited a manuscript. He clarifies that an initial part (from the Basmalah to a point) is not from the original author but was added for blessing. The passage concludes with bibliographic details about the Ottoman print. This is purely informational and does not contain legal rulings.

Key Terms
اللباب في شرح الكتاب (al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab): The title of the commentary authored by ‘Abd al-Ghani al-Maydani on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, meaning 'The Core in Explaining the Book'.
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A concise manual of Hanafi fiqh by Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), widely studied in the Hanafi madhhab.
مفتي زاده (Mufi Zadeh): Nickname of Muhammad al-Mukhtar al-Batumi, meaning 'son of the mufti', indicating his scholarly lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the scribe Hasan Shawqi, why did he include the passage from the Basmalah to the prayer in the manuscript?
A) He believed it was originally from al-Quduri.
B) He included it despite knowing it was not original, seeking blessing.
C) He was forced to include it by the publisher.
D) He included it because all copies had it.

Key Takeaway
Scribes like Hasan Shawqi provide critical notes that separate the original author's text from later pious additions, reminding scholars to verify the authenticity of what they read.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
Understanding the Editorial History of Al-Lubab Sharh al-Quduri
Describes editorial history and printed features

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of the muharrir (editor) in Hanafi printed texts and evaluate the authenticity of textual additions such as the basmala.

Core Idea
The basmala in al-Quduri is a later addition: not every text you read is exactly as the author wrote.

This passage from Al-Lubab Sharh al-Quduri provides a rare glimpse into the editorial process of a classical Hanafi text. It reveals how marginal notes (hawashi) were added, sources attributed, and even the opening basmala was recognized as a later insertion. Understanding these editorial interventions is crucial for anyone relying on printed editions for legal study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the editor's methodology
The editor states he adorned the margins with annotations, attributing every branch to its root and providing proofs. This shows a commitment to source documentation.
Look for the phrase 'attributing every branch to its root' which indicates the editor's method.
2
Recognize the acknowledgment of a non-original addition
A marginal note clarifies that the basmala and prayer at the beginning are not found in old copies and are not from the author, but were added for blessing.
This is a critical moment of textual honesty from the editor.
3
Define the term 'muharrir'
The text defines the muharrir as the one who prepares the book for printing, corrects it, and serves it. In this context, Hasan Shawqi is the muharrir.
The term appears in the note about the basmala.
4
Examine the printing and layout details
The book was printed in an Ottoman edition with a license date of December 1312. The layout places the original text in the body and marginal notes numbered and attributed to sources like 'Ayni' and 'Hidaya'.
Note the mention of lithographic printing style from Pakistan and India.
5
Consider the modern editorial issue
The author mentions receiving a recent photocopy from Turkey where the publisher deleted the author's name from the introduction, highlighting the need for careful verification of editions.
This underscores the importance of examining front matter.

Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
... its unique points, so I began to adorn the margins of its delicate pages with what is in reality the white of the page, attributing every branch to its root, and everything to its place, including proofs and evidences, and the explanations of issues. And Allah is sufficient for us and the best of trustees, and there is no power nor strength except by Allah, the Most High, the Great. End.
And written at the end of this copy: 'Written by the pen of the one in need of the kindness of his Guiding Lord: Hasan Shawqi bin Uthman al-Wahbi al-Hizar Ghradi, engaged in teaching sciences to students at the Fatih Mosque of Constantinople by the aid of the Bestower King, in the year 1315 AH, in (18) the month of his birth, peace be upon him.' End.
And this name 'Hasan Shawqi' was written after a marginal note between the lines placed on the Basmala and the prayer upon the Prophet (peace be upon him) that appeared at the beginning of the copy of al-Quduri. He wrote upon it the following:
'This expression from the Basmala to here is not found in the old copies, and its wording indicates that it is not from the speech of the author, may Allah have mercy on him, but we wrote it as we found it, seeking blessing. Written by its editor Hasan Shawqi.' End.
Therefore, what is meant by 'its editor' is the one who cared for printing the book, correcting it, and serving it. And Allah knows best.
And the book is printed in an Ottoman edition, and the date of obtaining the printing license was (11) December, year (1312), like that, although he mentioned the month of December?!
And the printed copy is rare; among them is a copy deposited in the library of Shaykh
Abd al-Qadir al-Shalabi, may Allah have mercy on him, preserved in the King Abdulaziz Library in Madinah al-Munawwarah, and I obtained a copy of it, praise be to Allah. And the marginal notes were connected with continuous numbers on each page with the original, i.e., Mukhtasar al-Quduri, which was placed in the body of the page, and each marginal note is attributed to its author in abbreviation, for example: 'Ayni', 'Hidaya', and so on. Also, marginal notes between the lines were written in the style of old lithographic printing in the presses of Pakistan and India, until the page became full, crowded, overflowing with its benefits.
In summary, the book is very beneficial, and it is a great effort in collecting these marginal notes and benefits from many sources, but it needs a bright print. And recently, I received this book from Turkey as a photocopy, dated 1398 AH, but its publisher deliberately deleted the author's name from the introduction?!
102 - Manhal al-Tullab Sharh al-Kitab (Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri) by the distinguished Hanafi jurist Abd al-Qadir bin Abdullah bin Hasan al-Ustawani al-Ansari al-Dimashqi, who died in 1314 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. And the author of 'Tarikh Ulama Dimashq' mentioned this book for him, and placed a picture of the title page and the first folio, in which it says: 'Its commencement was on the first of Jumada al-Awwal in the year 1274.' End.
(1) In the fourteenth century 131/1.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a description of the book 'Al-Lubab Sharh al-Quduri' and its editorial history. The author explains his methodology: he added marginal notes to the original text of Mukhtasar al-Quduri, attributing each sub-branch to its source and providing proofs and legal reasoning. A note at the end indicates that the copy was written by Hasan Shawqi in 1315 AH. Another note clarifies that the basmala and prayer from the beginning of al-Quduri's text are not original but were added by the editor for blessing. The book was printed in an Ottoman edition in 1312. The author obtained a rare printed copy from the library of Shaykh Abd al-Qadir al-Shalabi. The layout of the book places the original text in the body of the page with marginal notes numbered and attributed to sources like 'Ayni' and 'Hidaya', and also includes interlinear notes in lithographic style. Despite its many benefits, the author notes that the book needs a clearer print. He mentions receiving a recent photocopy from Turkey where the publisher omitted the author's name. Finally, he lists another work, 'Manhal al-Tullab Sharh al-Kitab', by Abd al-Qadir al-Ustawani, which is a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, begun in 1274 AH.

Key Terms
محرر (muharrir): The person who prepared the book for printing, corrected it, and served it (editor).
حاشية (hashiyah): Marginal notes added to the original text, often containing explanations, attributions, or additional rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They are original and written by al-Quduri.
B) They are not found in old copies and not from the author but added for blessing.
C) They were added by a later jurist for legal benefit.
D) They are authentic but were removed from later prints.

Key Takeaway
Always verify the authenticity of textual elements in printed Hanafi works by consulting front matter and editorial notes.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Three Hanafi Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
Listing and describing three commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three Hanafi commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri and state their significance in the Hanafi scholarly tradition.

Core Idea
The Mukhtasar of al-Quduri is the central matn of the Hanafi school, and these three commentaries exemplify the ongoing scholarly engagement with it.

This passage introduces three important Hanafi commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, a foundational text in the Hanafi madhhab. Understanding these works helps you trace the transmission and development of Hanafi fiqh from the classical period to the later scholars (mutakhkhirin). The passage also clarifies a key convention: when 'al-Kitab' is used absolutely in Hanafi circles, it refers specifically to al-Quduri's Mukhtasar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three commentaries
List the three titles, authors, and dates mentioned in the passage.
The first is 'Manhal al-Tullab li-Sharh al-Kitab', author unnamed but started at age 25 (b. 1249 AH). The second is 'al-Tanqih al-Daruri' by Nizam al-Din al-Kiranawi (d. after 1315 AH). The third is 'al-Mu'tasar al-Daruri' by Muhammad Sulayman al-Hindi (d. after 1320 AH).
2
Understand the significance of 'al-Kitab'
Note the author's statement: in the Hanafi madhhab, when the word 'al-Kitab' is used without qualification, it means Mukhtasar al-Quduri.
This convention shows the central status of al-Quduri's work in the school.
3
Analyze the nature of each commentary
Read the author's preface excerpts to understand each work's purpose: the first is a full commentary adding benefits from madhhab books; the second is a set of glosses (hawashi); the third is likely a similar exposition.
The differences reflect the various approaches to sharh (commentary) and hashiya (gloss) in the tradition.
4
Recognize the printing and manuscript history
Note that all three were printed lithographically in India/Pakistan, and the third was later printed in Karachi. The present author notes he could not locate the manuscript of the first in the Zahiriyya Library.
This highlights the challenges of manuscript access and the importance of printed editions for the survival of later Hanafi works.
5
Appreciate the scholarly chain
Reflect on how these commentaries connect to the earlier Hanafi tradition by citing 'the reliable books of the madhhab' and transmissions from trustworthy teachers.
These works are part of the later Hanafi tradition (fatawa mutakhkhirin), building upon the foundational matn and earlier commentaries.

Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
That the author's age was twenty-five years, as he was born in the year 1249 AH. And in the author's introduction: 'Praise be to Allah who is free from likenesses and parallels... As to after... Indeed, the book of al-Quduri has flown in the regions, traveled in the cities, and surpassed in fame the sun at midday, so that people devoted themselves to it and turned to it for recourse. I have spent a period of time laboring over it and exerted extreme effort until I collected a number of benefits and a delightful set of regulations. So I wanted to compile them on that book, adding to them from the reliable books of the madhhab, indicating them with what clarifies them. Whatever came from my own weak thought I would indicate, and whatever came from others I would point out. And I named it 'Manhal al-Tullab li-Sharh al-Kitab' because 'al-Kitab' when used absolutely in our madhhab refers to al-Quduri...' End. I have not found anything about the existence of this copy in the catalog of manuscripts of the Zahiriyya Library in Damascus, nor elsewhere, and Allah knows best.
103 - al-Tanqih al-Daruri fi Masa'il al-Quduri
By Muhammad called Nizam al-Din al-Kiranawi, he finished drafting it in the year 1315 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. And in his author's preface: '... After: the poor slave in need of Allah, the Rich, the Strong: Muhammad called Nizam al-Din al-Kiranawi, may Allah forgive his sins and conceal his faults: These are glosses I have attached to the strong text of the imam, the great, Abu al-Husayn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ja'far al-Quduri, may Allah elevate his ranks and multiply his good deeds, despite my limited capacity and scant knowledge, taking from the comprehensive books, transmitting from the reliable works, along with what came to me from what my trustworthy teachers benefited me. And I named them 'al-Tanqih al-Daruri fi Masa'il al-Quduri'...' End. The book was printed in India in several lithographic editions, in one volume of 296 pages, the sixth edition being in 1333 AH, according to the printing method in India and Pakistan, placing notes right and left, top and bottom, and in every corner of the pages.
104 - al-Mu'tasar al-Daruri 'ala Mukhtasar al-Quduri
By Muhammad Sulayman al-Hindi, who died after 1320 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. The drafting was completed in 1320 AH. The book was printed in India and Pakistan in several lithographic editions, including an edition in one volume of 335 pages in the Pakistani and Indian printing style. In this edition the author's name was not mentioned. However, the book was recently printed in the well-known Arab Eastern style in Karachi, under the care of Na'im Ashraf Nur Ahmad, who wrote that the author's name was not mentioned in the old Indian editions. He said: Then we came across his name in some comments at the end of which his name was written.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is biographical and bibliographical, listing three important Hanafi commentaries on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The first is 'Manhal al-Tullab li-Sharh al-Kitab', whose author began writing at age 25 (b. 1249 AH). He explains that his work is a commentary adding benefits from madhhab books. The second is 'al-Tanqih al-Daruri' by Nizam al-Din al-Kiranawi (d. after 1315 AH), a set of glosses (hawashi) on Quduri's text. The third is 'al-Mu'tasar al-Daruri' by Muhammad Sulayman al-Hindi (d. after 1320 AH). The passage notes printing history: all were lithographed in India/Pakistan, with the third later printed in Karachi. The author of the present book (likely al-Maydani, the commentator on al-Quduri) notes that he could not locate the manuscript of the first commentary in the Zahiriyya Library. These works are part of the later (mutakhkhirin) layer of Hanafi scholarship.

Key Terms
القدوري (al-Quduri): Abu al-Husayn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri, the Hanafi jurist, author of Mukhtasar al-Quduri, a foundational text in the Hanafi madhhab.
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): The abridged fiqh manual by al-Quduri, covering all major chapters of Hanafi fiqh.
حواش (hawashi): Glosses or supercommentaries, here referring to the work 'al-Tanqih al-Daruri' which adds explanatory notes to al-Quduri's text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
These three later commentaries illustrate the living tradition of scholarly engagement with Mukhtasar al-Quduri, the central matn of the Hanafi school.

Navigating Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
Bibliographic list of four commentaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four major commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri and explain their role in the Hanafi scholarly tradition.

Core Idea
Mukhtasar al-Quduri's commentaries are keys to understanding Hanafi fiqh transmission.

This lesson introduces you to a bibliographic list of commentaries on the foundational Hanafi text Mukhtasar al-Quduri. While not a legal ruling, such lists are vital for navigating the scholarly tradition. Knowing which commentary to consult helps you find authoritative explanations and trace the development of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Text
Recognize Mukhtasar al-Quduri as the concise fiqh manual by Abu al-Husayn al-Quduri. It is a core matn in the Hanafi school.
This text is widely studied and commented upon.
2
List the Commentaries
From the passage, extract the four commentaries: al-Tanqih al-Daruri, al-Mu'tasar al-Daruri, al-Hall al-Daruri, and al-Shihab fi Tawdih al-Kitab.
Each has a specific author and publication history.
3
Note the Authors and Dates
For each commentary, note the author's name and death date (if given). This helps situate the commentary in the Hanafi tradition.
Authors include Nizam al-Din al-Kiranawi, Muhammad Sulayman al-Hindi, Abd al-Hamid al-Laknawi, and Abd Allah Mustafa al-Maragi.
4
Understand the Printing History
Observe that these commentaries were printed in India/Pakistan in lithographic editions, often in the margin of Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Later editions follow Arab-Eastern style.
This reflects the global transmission of Hanafi texts.
5
Appreciate the Purpose of Such Lists
Bibliographic lists like this guide students and scholars to authoritative commentaries, ensuring correct understanding of the matn.
They are an essential part of the madrasa curriculum.

الفصل الثالث : شروح مختصر القدوري
محمد المدعو بنظام الدين الكيرانوي، غفر الله ذنوبه، وستر عيوبه : إن هذه حواش علقتها على المتن المتين، للإمام الهمام أبي الحسين أحمد بن محمد بن جعفر القدوري، أعلى الله درجاته، وضاعف حسناته مع قصور باعي، وقلة بضاعتي، آخذاً من المبسوطات، ناقلاً عن المعتبرات، مع ما حضـر لي مما أفادني ومشايخي الثقات، وسميتُها بـ «التنقيح الضروري في مسائل القدوري».....». اهـ وقد طبع الكتاب في الهند عدة طبعات حجرية، في مجلد يقع في (٢٩٦) صفحة، وكانت الطبعة السادسة سنة ١٣٣٣هـ، وذلك على طريقة الطباعة في الهند وباكستان، من وضع الحواشي يمنة ويسرة، وأسفل وأعلى، وفي كل زاوية من زوايا الصفحات.
١٠٤ ـ المعتصر الضروري على مختصر القدوري. لمحمد سليمان الهندي، المتوفى بعد سنة ١٣٢٠هـ، رحمه الله تعالى، وكان الانتهاء من تسويده سنة ١٣٢٠هـ. وقد طبع الكتاب في الهند وباكستان عدة طبعات حجرية، منها طبعة في مجلد يقع في (۳۳۵) صفحة على الطريقة الباكستانية والهندية في الطباعة، ولم يُذكر في هذه الطبعة اسم المؤلف، لكن طبع الكتاب حديثاً على الطريقة العربية المشرقية المشهورة، في كراتشي، باعتناء نعيم أشرف نور أحمد، وكتـب أنـه لم يُذكر في الطبعات الهندية القديمة اسم مؤلفه، يقول : ثم اطلعنا على اسمه في بعض التعليقات التي ذكر في آخرها اسمه. وصدرت هذه الطبعة في إدارة القرآن الكريم والعلوم الإسلامية، في كراتشي، ط١٤٢٩/٣هـ ، في مجلد كبير في (٧٨٠) صفحة.
١٠٥ - الحل الضروري لمختصر القدوري. للعلامة الفقيه الشيخ عبد الحميد بن عبد الحليم بن عبد الحكيم ابن عبد الرب الأنصاري اللكنوي، المتوفى سنة ١٣٥٣هـ، رحمه الله تعالى. ذكره، وترجم له عبد الحي الحسني في نزهة الخواطر، وهو مطبوع طبعة هندية حجرية بهامش مختصر القدوري (۲)
١٠٦- الشهاب في توضيح الكتاب، أو التعليقات المفيدة على متن القدوري. لعبد الله مصطفى المراغي، مدير قسم المساجد بوزارة الأوقاف بالقاهرة، وهو صاحب كتاب: الفتح المبين في طبقات الأصوليين»، (ت أواخر الرابع عشر الهجري)، واشترك معه في التأليف: عبد القادر يوسف، المدرس بمعهد القاهرة، وقد جاء في مقدمة ما كتباه: ...... وبعد : فهذه تعليقات واضحةٌ دَفَعَنا إلى تحريرها رغبتنا في معاونة طلاب الفقه المعنيين بدراسة هذا السفر الجليل المشهور بمختصر القدوري، لتكون مصباحاً لهم يهتدون به في تفهم معانيه والوصول إلى مراميه والله نسأل أن يجعل هذا العمل خالصاً لوجهه، .۲۲۸/۸ (۱)
Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
485
Muhammad, known as Nizam al-Din al-Kiranawi, may Allah forgive his sins and conceal his faults: These are glosses I wrote on the solid text of the imam, the great Abu al-Husayn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ja'far al-Quduri, may Allah elevate his ranks and multiply his good deeds, despite my limited capacity and meager knowledge, taking from the expansive works, transmitting from reliable sources, along with what was presented to me from what my trustworthy teachers benefited me, and I named them 'al-Tanqih al-Daruri fi Masa'il al-Quduri'... end quote. The book was printed in India in several lithographic editions, in one volume of 296 pages, and the sixth edition was in 1333 AH, in the printing style of India and Pakistan, placing glosses on all sides.
104 - al-Mu'tasar al-Daruri 'ala Mukhtasar al-Quduri. By Muhammad Sulayman al-Hindi, who died after 1320 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. Completion of writing was in 1320 AH. The book was printed in India and Pakistan in several lithographic editions, including an edition in one volume of 335 pages in the Pakistani and Indian printing style, and the author's name was not mentioned in this edition, but the book was printed recently in the well-known Arab-Eastern style in Karachi, under the care of Na'im Ashraf Nur Ahmad, and he wrote that the author's name was not mentioned in old Indian editions; he says: then we found his name in some notes at the end of which his name was mentioned. This edition was published by Idarat al-Quran wa al-Ulum al-Islamiyya in Karachi, 1st edition 1429/3 AH, in one large volume of 780 pages.
105 - al-Hall al-Daruri li-Mukhtasar al-Quduri. By the distinguished jurist Shaykh 'Abd al-Hamid ibn 'Abd al-Halim ibn 'Abd al-Hakim ibn 'Abd al-Rabb al-Ansari al-Laknawi, who died in 1353 AH, may Allah have mercy on him. He was mentioned and his biography was written by 'Abd al-Hayy al-Hasani in Nuzhat al-Khawatir, and it is printed as a lithographic Indian edition in the margin of Mukhtasar al-Quduri (2)
106 - al-Shihab fi Tawdih al-Kitab, or al-Ta'liqat al-Mufida 'ala Matn al-Quduri. By 'Abdullah Mustafa al-Maragi, director of the Department of Mosques of the Ministry of Awqaf in Cairo, and he is the author of the book al-Fath al-Mubin fi Tabaqat al-Usuliyyin, (died late 14th century AH). He was joined in authorship by 'Abd al-Qadir Yusuf, teacher at the Cairo Institute. In the introduction of what they wrote: ... After this, these are clear glosses that we were motivated to write by our desire to assist students of fiqh concerned with studying this noble work famous as Mukhtasar al-Quduri, to be a lamp for them guiding them in understanding its meanings and reaching its goals. We ask Allah to make this work purely for His sake.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographic listing from the third chapter of al-Lubab Sharh al-Quduri, which enumerates commentaries written on the foundational Hanafi text Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The author of al-Lubab, 'Abd al-Ghani al-Maydani, includes this as front matter. The passage describes four works: (1) al-Tanqih al-Daruri by Nizam al-Din al-Kiranawi, a set of glosses; (2) al-Mu'tasar al-Daruri by Muhammad Sulayman al-Hindi, a concise commentary; (3) al-Hall al-Daruri by 'Abd al-Hamid al-Laknawi; and (4) al-Shihab fi Tawdih al-Kitab by 'Abdullah Mustafa al-Maragi and 'Abd al-Qadir Yusuf. For each, it provides author, publication details, and printing history. This is not a legal ruling but rather a scholarly apparatus showing the transmission and study of the Quduri text. Students should note that such lists are crucial for identifying authoritative commentaries within the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): The concise fiqh manual by Abu al-Husayn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), a foundational text in the Hanafi school.
التنقيح الضروري في مسائل القدوري (al-Tanqih al-Daruri fi Masa'il al-Quduri): Glosses on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Nizam al-Din al-Kiranawi.
المعتصر الضروري على مختصر القدوري (al-Mu'tasar al-Daruri 'ala Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A concise commentary by Muhammad Sulayman al-Hindi.

The First Edition of Al-Lubab: A Pedagogical Masterpiece
Describes the publication details of a Hanafi commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the layout and pedagogical design of the first edition of Al-Lubab and explain how it served as a graded textbook for Azhar students.

Core Idea
Al-Lubab’s layout—matn at top, commentary in footnotes—maximized learning for Azhar students.

This passage describes the first edition of a major Hanafi commentary. Understanding its structure reveals how classical scholars designed text and commentary to build student proficiency incrementally, a model still relevant today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the publication details
Note the year (1368 AH / 1948 CE), publisher (Mustafa al-Babi al-Halabi, Cairo), and the author’s prayer for benefit.
This establishes the work as a modern printed edition rooted in tradition.
2
Analyze the layout design
The matn (original text) is placed at the top of the page, fully voweled. The commentary appears in footnotes as brief explanatory sentences.
This design prioritizes the matn as the core text.
3
Recognize the pedagogical purpose
The work is divided into three volumes, each assigned to a specific year of the Azhar elementary curriculum (years 2, 3, and 4).
This indicates a graded, sequential study plan.
4
Connect to the broader Hanafi tradition
Relate this layout to the classical sharh tradition where commentaries expand and clarify a matn without replacing it.
Al-Lubab exemplifies the Hanafi method of incremental learning.

وأن ينفع به كما نفع بأصله، إنه سميع مجيب. غُـرة المحرم، سنة ١٣٦٨ هـ ـ نوفمبر سنة ١٩٤٨م . اهـ

Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. And may He benefit through it as He benefited through its original; indeed He is All-Hearing, All-Answering. Beginning of Muharram, year 1368 AH - November 1948 CE. End. The first edition was published in the year 1368 AH, at Mustafa al-Babi al-Halabi Press in Cairo. The text of al-Quduri was placed in full at the top of the page, fully voweled, and comments were placed in the margin. They are very subtle benefits, as they consist of short sentences to clarify the text. It was printed in three parts: the first part in (91) pages, which is the fiqh curriculum for second-year students of the elementary division of the religious institutes at Al-Azhar University. The second part in (147) pages, which is the curriculum for the third year of the elementary division of the religious institutes at Al-Azhar University. And the third part in (213) pages, which is the curriculum for the fourth year.

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographical description of a Hanafi commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri, likely Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri by Al-Maydani. The author begins with a prayer that the work be beneficial, then gives the publication date (Muharram 1368 AH / November 1948 CE). The first edition was published in Cairo by Mustafa al-Babi al-Halabi. The layout places the original matn (text of al-Quduri) at the top of the page with full vowel markings, and the commentary in footnotes. The footnotes are described as 'lata'if' (subtle points) consisting of short explanatory sentences. The work was printed in three volumes, each designated for a specific year of the Azhar elementary curriculum: volume 1 for year 2, volume 2 for year 3, and volume 3 for year 4. This shows the work was designed as a graded textbook for students, indicating its pedagogical importance in the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): The foundational Hanafi fiqh primer by Abul-Husayn al-Quduri, used as the base text for this commentary.
متن (matn): The original text of al-Quduri placed at the top of the page in this edition.

Core Idea
Mukhtasar al-Quduri's enduring centrality is evident in its vast commentary tradition across the Hanafi world.

This passage from Al-Lubab presents a bibliographical survey of commentaries on the foundational Hanafi text Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Understanding this tradition helps students appreciate the depth of scholarship and choose appropriate explanatory works for their studies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main text and its role
Recognize Mukhtasar al-Quduri as the base matn (text) of Hanafi fiqh, and note that it is the object of the commentaries listed.
This text is a concise summary of Hanafi rulings by Imam al-Quduri.
2
Understand the source work
The passage is from Al-Lubab by al-Maydani, a commentary itself. The author lists other commentaries to show the breadth of the tradition.
Al-Maydani's work is a standard reference for Mukhtasar al-Quduri.
3
Read each entry systematically
Go through each numbered entry: note the title, author (if known), library location, manuscript number, and any condition (e.g., incomplete).
There are six entries: Majma' al-Adillah, Tanwir al-Qulub, Sharh by Husayn ibn Abdullah, Al-Takmilah, an anonymous Sharh, and a Persian translation.
4
Recognize the geographical spread
Observe that libraries are in Istanbul, Tunisia, Mecca, and Samsun, indicating the wide geographic circulation of these works.
This shows the trans-regional study of Hanafi fiqh.

Note that some authors are unknown or have lost biographies, yet their works are preserved. This highlights the collective effort of the Hanafi tradition.
Even incomplete manuscripts are valued.
6

The Scholarly Reception of Mukhtasar al-Quduri
Bibliography of translations and commentaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main translations of Mukhtasar al-Quduri and explain why this text is foundational in the Hanafi madhhab.

Core Idea
Mukhtasar al-Quduri is the gateway to Hanafi fiqh — its translations show its global scholarly impact.

This lesson introduces a bibliographic appendix from al-Maydani's commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It lists translations and commentaries into Turkish, German, and French, revealing the text's reach beyond the Arab world. Understanding the reception of this core manual helps us appreciate the transmission of Hanafi legal knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main text
The passage discusses Mukhtasar al-Quduri, a concise Hanafi fiqh manual by Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri. Recognise it as a foundational text for beginners in the Hanafi school.
The manual covers essential rulings in ibadat, muamalat, and other chapters.
2
Categorise the listings
The passage lists both full translations and partial ones (e.g., Book of Jihad, Book of Nikah). Distinguish between commentaries (shuruh) and translations (tarajim).
A commentary explains the text; a translation transfers it into another language.
3
Analyse the languages
The translations are into Turkish, German, and French. Turkish reflects Ottoman interest; German and French show European Orientalist engagement.
This multilingual reception indicates the text's importance beyond the Arabic-speaking world.
4
Note the scholarly references
The author cites Brockelmann's Geschichte der Arabischen Literatur and other sources. This demonstrates academic rigour in documenting the text's circulation.
Such referencing is common in scholarly bibliographies to establish credibility.
5
Reflect on the author's attitude
The author concludes that there may be other works not yet discovered, expressing humility and ongoing scholarly pursuit.
This attitude reflects the traditional adab of scholarship: acknowledging the limits of one's knowledge.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate

Verifying Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri: Manuscript Attribution
Listing and verifying commentaries on al-Quduri's work

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify common errors in manuscript attribution and apply criteria to verify whether a commentary is correctly assigned to a Hanafi scholar.

Core Idea
A commentary cannot be attributed to a scholar who died before the base text was written — chronology is the first check.

This passage from Lubab Sharh al-Quduri illustrates the meticulous work of verifying manuscript attributions. The author, al-Maidani, corrects several misattributions, showing how historical critics used chronology, biography, and manuscript examination to ensure texts are correctly linked to their authors. Understanding this process is essential for anyone studying classical Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text and its author
Recognize that the core text is Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Abu al-Husayn Ahmad al-Quduri, who died in 428 AH. This is the reference point for all commentaries discussed.
Al-Quduri wrote his Mukhtasar around 420 AH, near the end of his life.
2
List each claimed commentary and attributed author
From the passage, note three commentaries: (1) attributed to al-Nabulsi, (2) attributed to al-Karkhi, (3) attributed to al-Isbijabi. Also mention the commentary by al-Kirmani that is correctly cited.
The author also corrects a misattribution to Abd al-Ghani al-Maidani.
3
Apply chronological verification
Check the death date of each attributed author against the composition date of Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Al-Karkhi died around 410 AH, before the Mukhtasar was written, so he cannot have commented on it.
Al-Nabulsi lived much later (died 1143 AH), so a commentary by him is chronologically possible, but the manuscript turned out to be a copy of the matn, not a commentary.
4
Examine biographical expertise
Investigate whether the attributed author is known as a faqih (jurist). Al-Karkhi's biographies mention only mathematics, not fiqh, making a fiqh commentary unlikely. Al-Isbijabi was a judge, so a commentary is plausible but in fact his work is on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, not al-Quduri.
Specialization in a field is a strong indicator of a scholar's likely contributions.
5
Verify through manuscript features
Physical examination of the manuscript (script size, spacing, colophon, date) can reveal whether it is a commentary or a copy of the matn. The al-Nabulsi manuscript had large script and wide spacing, typical of a matn copy, and was dated 1104 AH, fitting a copy rather than a commentary.
Librarians and indexers sometimes mislabel manuscripts based on cover inscriptions or catalog errors.

النابلسي حياته وآراؤه»(۱)، وذكر نسخة الظاهرية السابقة. وقد كنت في شك كبير من نسبة هذا الشرح للنابلسي، وسعيتُ كثيراً للكشف عن حال هذه النسخة حتى يسر الله لي الاتصال بالأخ الكريم الفاضل الأستاذ الباحث عمر نشوقاتي في دمشق، فنظر فيها، وأزال لي الشك باليقين، وأخبرني - جزاه الله خيراً ـ أنها نسخة من مختصر القدوري، وأن حرفها كبير، وبين السطور فراغ كبير، مما جعل عدد أوراقها كثيراً (۳۰۰) ورقة وأنها كتبت سنة ١١٠٤هـ. وقد وقع المفهرسون في خطأ آخر أيضاً، حيث كتبوا على غلافها: «شرح الشيخ عبد الغني الميداني على مختصر القدوري». ه شرح مختصر القدوري، لأبي بكر محمد بن الحسن الكرخي، المتوفى نحو سنة ٤١٠هـ.
(۲) نسبه له أصحاب الفهرس الشامل على أنه من شروح القدوري، وأن منه نسخة في المركز الحكومي باسطنبول، برقم (٢٠٥). وقد ترجم للمؤلف الزركلي في الأعلام، وأنه ترجمه بعضهم وجعل لقبه «الكرجي»: بالجيم. ومما يجعل النفس تميل إلى بعد نسبة هذا الشرح لهذا المؤلف، أنه لم يُذكر في ترجمته بفقه أبداً، لا حنفي ولا غيره، بل ذكرت له كتب في الرياضيات والجبر والهندسة. وتقدم أن القدوري ألف مختصره في أواخر عمره، في نحو سنة ٤٢٠هـ، مما يؤكد بعد تأليف المذكور لشرح على القدوري.

The Third Section: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri
Al-Nabulsi: His Life and Opinions, and the previously mentioned copy of al-Zahiriyya. I had great doubt about the attribution of this commentary to al-Nabulsi, and I strove much to uncover the condition of this copy until Allah facilitated contact with the noble brother, the researcher Mr. Umar Nashuqati in Damascus. He examined it and removed my doubt with certainty, and informed me—may Allah reward him—that it is a copy of Mukhtasar al-Quduri, its script is large, and there is much empty space between the lines, making the number of folios many (300 folios), and it was written in the year 1104 AH. The indexers also made another mistake: they wrote on its cover: 'The Commentary of Sheikh Abd al-Ghani al-Maidani on Mukhtasar al-Quduri.' This is the commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Karkhi, who died around the year 410 AH.
(2) The indexers of the Comprehensive Index attributed it to him as one of the commentaries on al-Quduri, and that a copy exists in the Government Center in Istanbul, number 205. Al-Zirikli translated the author in al-Alam, and some translated him and gave his epithet as 'al-Karji' (with jim). What makes one incline to reject attribution of this commentary to this author is that he was never mentioned in his biography as a jurist (faqih) at all—neither Hanafi nor otherwise—rather, books on mathematics, algebra, and geometry were mentioned for him. And it was previously stated that al-Quduri wrote his Mukhtasar towards the end of his life, around the year 420 AH, which confirms the improbability that the aforementioned person wrote a commentary on al-Quduri.
6- The Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Judge Abu Nasr Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Isbijabi, who died in 481 AH. Brockelmann attributed it to him in Geschichte der Arabischen Litteratur, and mentioned that a copy exists in the Damadzada (Murad Mulla) library, number 904. Upon examining this copy, it turned out to be an old copy, partly worm-eaten, consisting of 184 folios, and the book is not a commentary on al-Quduri, but rather a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi. The author mentioned in the introduction that al-Quduri wrote a commentary on it and expanded it, but he himself wanted brevity and conciseness.
- The Clarification of Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Rukn al-Din Abul Fadl al-Kirmani Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn Amiruyah, who died in 543 AH.)
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographical discussion from the introductory section of 'Lubab Sharh al-Quduri' by al-Maidani. The author is documenting various commentaries on the foundational Hanafi fiqh text, Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The author first addresses a misattributed commentary erroneously linked to al-Nabulsi, clarifying that the manuscript in question is actually a copy of Mukhtasar al-Quduri itself, not a commentary. He also corrects another misattribution to Abd al-Ghani al-Maidani, showing it is actually by al-Karkhi. The author notes the improbability of al-Karkhi commenting on al-Quduri given that al-Karkhi died before al-Quduri wrote his Mukhtasar. Next, the author discusses the commentary by al-Isbijabi, which is not on al-Quduri but on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi. Finally, he mentions the commentary by al-Kirmani. This section demonstrates careful manuscript verification and correction of scholarly errors. It is useful for students to understand the history and authenticity of Hanafi fiqh texts.

Key Terms
شرح (sharh): A commentary that explains and expands upon a base text (matn). Here, commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri.
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A concise Hanafi fiqh manual by Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Quduri (d. 428 AH), foundational for the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Verifying manuscript attributions requires cross-referencing dates, biographies, and physical manuscript evidence; a good historian of fiqh must be a detective.

Verifying Commentaries: Distinguishing Mukhtasar al-Quduri from Mukhtasar al-Karkhi
Identifying that commentaries by al-Ispijabi and al-Kirmani are actually on al-Karkhi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a commentary attributed to Mukhtasar al-Quduri is actually on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, and explain the method of manuscript verification used.

Core Idea
Not every 'Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri' is on al-Quduri — verify through author's own introduction.

This passage corrects a common misattribution in Hanafi bibliography. Two well-known commentaries were thought to explain al-Quduri's primer, but manuscript evidence shows they actually explain al-Karkhi's earlier work. Understanding this helps us trace the development of Hanafi texts and avoid citation errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimants
Note that Brockelmann, Sezgin, and others attribute both works as commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. Recognize the two commentators: al-Ispijabi (d. 481 AH) and al-Kirmani (d. 543 AH).
2
Locate the manuscript evidence
The author refers to specific library copies: al-Ispijabi's at Damadzada (Murad Mulla) and al-Kirmani's at Fatih Library. These are physical sources to be examined.
Library numbers are given for traceability.
3
Examine the author's own words
For al-Kirmani, a direct quote from his introduction is provided. He states he commented on al-Karkhi's work and named it al-Idah, then later made an abridgment. This is the decisive evidence.
The introduction is the primary source for attribution.
4
Draw the conclusion
Both commentaries are actually on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, not al-Quduri. The titles are misleading; the internal evidence overrides external listings.
Always prioritize the author's statement over later catalogues.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وتقدم أن القدوري ألف مختصره في أواخر عمره، في نحو سنة ٤٢٠هـ، مما يؤكد بعد تأليف المذكور لشرح على القدوري.

٦- شرح مختصر القدوري للقاضي أبي نصر أحمد بن منصور الإسبيجابي، المتوفى سنة ٤٨١هـ.
نسبه له بروكلمان في تاريخ الأدب العربي، وذكر أنه منه نسخة في مكتبة داماد زاده (مراد ملا)، برقم (٩٠٤). (Y).
وبالاطلاع على هذه النسخة (۳) ، تبيَّن أنها نسخة قديمة، وقد أكل الدود بعضها، وهي في (١٨٤) ورقة والكتاب ليس شرحاً على القدوري، إنما هو شرح على مختصر الكرخي، وقد ذكر في المقدمة أن القدوري شرحه وأطال في شرحه، لكنه أراد هو الإيجاز والاختصار.
- إيضاح مختصر القدوري لركن الدين أبي الفضل الكرماني عبد الرحمن بن محمد بن أميرويه، المتوفى سنة ٥٤٣هـ ) (۳) .

الفصل الثالث : شروح مختصر القدوري (1)
٤٩٩
ذكره له سزكين في تاريخ التراث العربي، وعزاه الملحق بروكلمان، وأن منه نسخة في الخالدية القدس، برقم (١٠٨٥) كتبت سنة ٩٧٩هـ. (۲)
وذكره أصحاب الفهرس الشامل ( باسم : «الإيضاح في شرح القدوري»، وأن منه نسخة في مكتبة الفاتح باسطنبول، برقم (۱۷۸۰)، كتبت سنة ٥٢٧هـ.
وبالاطلاع على نُسَخ الكتاب تبيَّن أن الكتاب ليس شرحاً على القدوري، بل هو شرح على مختصر الكرخي.
وقد كتب إلي بهذا الأستاذ محمد فاتح قايا من اسطنبول، وقد نقل لي مقدمة نسخة مكتبة الفاتح، وأنه جاء في مقدمتها :
.... إني لما طالعت تصنيف الشيخ أبي الحسن الكرخي رحمه الله، ووجدته على أحسن صيغة وترتيب ومسائل أبوابه متجانسة، عمدت إلى شرح معانيها، وذكرت له عللاً مؤثرة، وسميته بـ: «الإيضاح»، ثم سألني بعض إخواني أن أفرد مسائل هذا الكتاب، وأقتصر على الروايات المعتمدة، ليقرب متناولها وحفظها، فأجبته إلى ذلك، طالباً من الله تعالى التوفيق». اهـ
وعلى هذا يكون (الإيضاح شرحاً على مختصر الكرخي، ثم أفرد مختصراً له، والواقع أن هذه النسخة سقط منها سطر سبب هذا .

Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. He was never mentioned in his biography as a jurist at all, neither Hanafi nor otherwise, rather books in mathematics, algebra, and geometry were mentioned for him. It was previously stated that al-Quduri authored his Mukhtasar at the end of his life, around the year 420 AH, which confirms the later authorship of the mentioned commentary on al-Quduri. 6. Commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri by the judge Abu Nasr Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Ispijabi, died 481 AH. Brockelmann attributed it to him in History of Arabic Literature, and mentioned that a manuscript exists in Damadzada Library (Murad Mulla), number 904. (Y). Upon examining this manuscript (3), it became clear that it is an old copy, some of it eaten by worms, and it is in 184 folios, and the book is not a commentary on al-Quduri, but rather a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, and he mentioned in the introduction that al-Quduri explained it and elaborated in his commentary, but he himself intended brevity and conciseness. - Idah Mukhtasar al-Quduri by Rukn al-Din Abu al-Fadl al-Kirmani Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn Amiruyah, died 543 AH) (3). 270/3 (1) (2) And Professor Muhammad Fatih Qaya undertook that, and wrote to me from Istanbul about the manuscript, and transmitted to me the author's introduction, may Allah reward him well. (3) His biography is in Taj al-Tarajim p. 184, al-Jawahir al-Mudiyya 2/388. Condition. Chapter Three: Commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri (1) 499. Sezgin mentioned it in History of Arabic Heritage, and attributed it to Brockelmann's supplement, and that a manuscript exists in al-Khalidiyya al-Quds, number 1085, written in 979 AH. (2) And the authors of al-Fihris al-Shamil mentioned it under the name 'al-Idah fi Sharh al-Quduri', and that a manuscript exists in Fatih Library in Istanbul, number 1780, written in 527 AH. Upon examining the copies of the book, it became clear that the book is not a commentary on al-Quduri, but rather a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi. And Professor Muhammad Fatih Qaya wrote to me about this from Istanbul, and he transmitted to me the introduction of the Fatih Library copy, and that it says in its introduction: '... I, when I studied the work of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, may Allah have mercy on him, and found it in the best style and arrangement, and the issues of its chapters are homogeneous, I set out to explain their meanings, and I mentioned effective causes for it, and I named it al-Idah, then some of my brothers asked me to isolate the issues of this book, and restrict myself to the reliable transmissions, to make it easy to access and memorize, so I responded to that, seeking success from Allah Almighty.' End quote. Thus, al-Idah is a commentary on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, then he isolated a summary for it, and in reality this copy has a missing line that caused this. 118/3 (1) 828/1 (2)
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a front matter discussion from a work listing commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The author is examining two works that are often listed as commentaries on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The first is by al-Ispijabi (d. 481 AH) and the second by al-Kirmani (d. 543 AH). Through manuscript examination and study of their introductions, it is determined that both are actually commentaries on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, not al-Quduri. The author notes that Brockelmann attributed the first to al-Ispijabi and that Sezgin and the comprehensive index listed the second as 'al-Idah fi Sharh al-Quduri', but the evidence from the manuscripts shows they are on al-Karkhi. The author relies on correspondence with Professor Muhammad Fatih Qaya who examined the manuscripts and confirmed they are commentaries on al-Karkhi. The passage is important for correcting the attribution of these works in the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
مختصر القدوري (Mukhtasar al-Quduri): A famous Hanafi fiqh primer by Imam al-Quduri (d. 428 AH)
مختصر الكرخي (Mukhtasar al-Karkhi): A Hanafi fiqh work by Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi (d. 340 AH)
الإيضاح (al-Idah): Title of a commentary by al-Kirmani, thought to be on al-Quduri but actually on al-Karkhi

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the actual subject of al-Kirmani's commentary 'al-Idah'?
A) Mukhtasar al-Quduri
B) Mukhtasar al-Karkhi
C) Jami' al-Kabir
D) Al-Hidayah

Key Takeaway
Two commentaries attributed to Mukhtasar al-Quduri are actually on Mukhtasar al-Karkhi; always verify through the author's own words.
